OHPC GRADE 6 SCIENCE
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Introduction
The study of science encompasses knowledge, processes and values. Scientifically literate persons will foster an attitude of caring not only for themselves,
but as responsible citizens, for the world around them. Their decision-making will be enhanced by a systematic study of the structure and behaviour of
the physical and natural world through observation and experiment. In learning science, learners benefit from leveraging and evaluating available
technological tools to study and therefore understand the world and their relationship to it.
The following Innovation Configuration Map is designed to assist teachers as they continue to develop the pedagogy that supports the curriculum. It
illustrates the key features of research informed best practices that might serve as a guide to teachers as they seek to engage with the curriculum with the
goal of having “Every Learner Succeed”.

Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Structure and Properties of Matter
Introduction to the Subject: The study of science encompasses knowledge, processes and values. Scientifically literate persons will foster an attitude of caring not
only for themselves, but as responsible citizens, for the world around them. Their decision making will be enhanced by a systematic study of the structure and
behaviour of the physical and natural world through observation and experiment. In learning science, Learners benefit from leveraging and evaluating available
technological tools to study and therefore understand the world and their relationship to it
Strand (Topic): Structure and Properties of Matter:
An understanding of how matter is constructed allows us to build molecules that are useful to humans.
Essential Learning Outcome: ELO 1: Develop models to describe the atomic composition of simple molecules and extended structures.
Clarification Statement: Emphasis is on developing models of molecules that vary in complexity. Examples of simple molecules could include ammonia and
methanol. Examples of extended structures could include sodium chloride or diamonds. Examples of molecular-level models could include drawings, 3D ball and
stick structures, or computer representations showing different molecules with different types of atoms.
Assessment Boundary: Assessment does not include valence electrons and bonding energy, discussing the ionic nature of subunits of complex structures, or a
complete depiction of all individual atoms in a complex molecule or extended structure.
Grade Level Expectations: Refer to grade level expectations at the beginning of this curriculum document.

Atoms Combine to Form Molecules!
Explain to Learners that atoms can be combined in many different
ways to make new substances called molecules. The atoms are
joined together by something called bonds.
Just like you have a bond or connection to your brother, sister,
mother or father, atoms are bonded together to form molecules.
Building Models
Now Learners, we are going to use marshmallows (atoms) and
toothpicks (bonds) to make some simple molecules.” The
number of atoms in a molecule is called its molecular formula.
E.g. the molecule hydrogen peroxide H O has 2 hydrogen
2 2
atoms and 2 oxygen atoms-note: we don’t usually write “1” in
our formulae so CO is carbon monoxide and it has 1 carbon
and 1 oxygen atom.
Teacher Notes:
a. You could also coffee sticks and plasticine balls if
available.
b. Choose marshmallows or plasticine of different colors
for different types of atoms
The teacher will now distribute marshmallows and toothpicks so
that children can build the molecules along with the teacher as the
teacher demonstrates and poses questions.

Models of Extended Structures
Some molecules have different types of bonding that allows them
to form what are called lattice structures. Two such examples are
NaCl and diamond.

Retrieved from: https://www.physics-in-a-
nutshell.com/article/13/diamond-structure
Because the carbon is bonded in so many directions, it makes
diamond one of the hardest things on earth!
Graphite is another carbon molecule with an unusual structure.
Based on this structure, why do you think it is in effective
lubricant?
The carbon layers are only weakly attracted and so the flat layers
can easily roll over each other. This makes graphite a good choice
for lubricating such things as locks. Its flexibility in one direction
and strength in another makes it useful for making handles and
shafts for sports items. (e.g. golf clubs and field hockey sticks)
Retrieved from: https://www.mechdaily.com/what-is-graphite/

Opportunities for Subject Integration:
Mathematics: Spatial awareness and the atom/calculating atomic charges/3d geometry of molecules
Language Arts: new terminology/research/communication of research
Social studies: History of atomic theory led to the atomic bomb & warfare decisions
Instructional Technology: Use of simulations for learning
TVET: Inventions that capitalize on the understanding of molecular properties e.g. diamond, graphite, carbon fibre, graphene, composites
Elements from Local Culture, Technology, TVET, Environment that are integrated:
Local Culture
● Several of these molecules are used in our everyday lives (e.g. ammonia for cleaning, methane as a fuel, hydrogen peroxide in hair bleach and
as an antiseptic.
● Salt harvesting from the Salt ponds in Union Island.
● Gases emitted from the volcanic eruption and from sulfur springs.
Environment
• Some of the molecules make up substances commonly found in the environment (e.g. carbon dioxide, water vapour, diamond, sodium chloride, etc.
Agriculture
• Methane produced from animal waste and used as a biofuel.
Items of Inspiration (teaching tips, inspirational passages, connections to educational research):
Understanding rudimentary atomic theory allows the student to predict reactivity and build strategies for making novel molecules – careers in chemistry

Essential Learning Outcome: ELO 2: Gather and make sense of information to describe that synthetic materials come from natural resources and
impact society
Clarification Statement: Emphasis is on natural resources that undergo a chemical process to form the synthetic material. Examples of new materials could
include new medicine, foods, and alternative fuels.
Assessment Boundary: Assessment is limited to qualitative information.
Grade Level Expectations: Refer to grade level expectations at the beginning of this curriculum document.

Making a Polymer Plastic from Milk: Classroom
Demonstration by the Teacher and/or an experiment for Learners
if resources are available
See instructional video here for materials and process.
https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=QulxqE4XY9M (5:06 mins)
Materials needed: milk, vinegar, sieve, paper towel, hotplate or
microwave
Instructions:
1. Heat 1 cup of 3% homogenised milk on a hot plate or in
the microwave until it bubbles
2. Add the hot milk to 1 tbsp (15 mL) of vinegar in a large
bowl
3. Pour the curdled mixture into a sieve lined with paper
towels
4. Allow the white solid to dry in the sieve for ~30 mins.
Squeeze the solid in a ball between paper towels to further remove
water.
5. Flatten out the dried solid and press it with a cookie
cutter.
6. Place the pressed-out shape in the window for 24 hours to
harden.
You have demonstrated with children that the plastics can be bent
and shaped. You have made a natural polymer plastic from a
protein found in milk called Casein.
The combination of heat and vinegar (acetic acid) helps to pull out
or precipitate the protein casein from the milk. Plastic is now
synthesized from petroleum products but before this way of
making plastic, it was common to make natural plastics from plants
and animal fat.
Making a Polymer from White Glue (teacher demonstration)
In a small glass jar, combine 2 tablespoons of Borax cleaning
powder and 10mL of water/stir.
22
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
In another small jar, place 2 tablespoons of white carpenter’s glue.
Pour the Borax slurry/solution into the jar of glue and stir with a
wooden coffee stick. As you stir the glue will change consistency
from a liquid to a rubbery polymer. Decant the liquid and rinse off
the rubbery glob with water. Dry it off and pass around to children
to see the new polymer.
Useful Polymers Have a Cost (teacher demonstration)
Disposable diapers contain a synthetic polymer called sodium
polyacrylate. It is a polymer that is able to trap up to 30 times its
weight in water.
Over a newspaper on the floor, a teacher can open the cotton in
the middle of the diaper and, running it through their hands, they
will see small sand-like pebbles of the polymer fall on the
newspaper. Gathering the powder up in a jar, the teacher can add
ml volumes of water and with stir note the powder forming a gel.
The capacity of the powder/gel to absorb water will amaze the
Learners. While this technology has been an enormous
convenience for parents, this synthetic invention is said to take 500
years to biodegrade in a landfill site!

Additional Useful Content Knowledge for the Teacher:
Core Ideas for meeting specific curriculum outcomes. Matter of any type can be subdivided into particles that are too small to see, but even then, the matter
still exists and can be detected by other means (e.g., by weighing or by its effects on other objects). For example, a model showing that gases are made from
matter particles that are too small to see and are moving freely around in space can explain many observations, including the inflation and shape of a balloon; the
23
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
effects of air on larger particles or objects (e.g., leaves in wind, dust suspended in air); and the appearance of visible scale water droplets in condensation, fog, and,
by extension, also in clouds or the contrails of a jet.
National Academies of Sciences, Engineering, and Medicine. 2012. A Framework for K12 Science Education: Practices, Crosscutting Concepts, and Core Ideas.
Washington, DC: The National Academies Press. https://doi.org/10.17226/13165.
Opportunities for Subject Integration:
Science: Discuss the chemical processes involved in turning natural resources into synthetic materials. You can use simple examples like rubber, plastics, and
medications.
Social Studies: Explore how synthetic materials have impacted Caribbean economies, like the role of oil refineries or agriculture and the use of fertilizers.
Mathematics: Have Learners calculate how much of a natural resource is needed to produce a certain quantity of synthetic material or explore the cost/benefit
analysis of using synthetic vs. natural resources.
Environmental Studies: Discuss the environmental effects of synthetic materials like plastic waste and the impact on marine life in the Caribbean.
Elements from Local Culture
● Vincentian farmers, especially in the rural area make coalpits to create charcoal which is used for fuel in outdoor cooking.
● Also in the rural areas, coconuts are green gold as they are used to make a variety of products i.e. coconut oil, coconut sugar cakes, bottled coconut water
● Some traditional natural remedies have been synthesised into modern medicines (e.g., the use of plants like neem or noni, aloe).
● Some of the traditional materials used in the home like crocus/plant based bags, gourd bowls, bamboo spoons, coconut fibres scrubbers are now being
replaced with products made from synthesised materials.
Environment
● Air masses and air pressure systems greatly influence the Caribbean weather. We have a known hurricane season because of this.

Technology
● How is technology used to make synthetic products.(How Its Made tv series. See:

TVET and Technology
● Incorporate local industries that rely on synthetic materials, such as manufacturing or oil refining. TVET programs that focus on these fields can offer
insight into local job markets and skills development.
Agriculture
● Synthetic fertilisers have impacted local Caribbean agriculture. Discuss natural vs. synthetic fertilizers and their effects on soil health and crop yields.

Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Cultural Impact:
● Discuss how Caribbean culture interacts with synthetic materials, such as the replacement of traditional materials in clothing or household items with
synthetic alternatives. Explore the cultural significance of this shift, both positive and negative.
● Use timelines and or photos to show the changes in the materials used to make Carnival costumes on your island. Discuss the advantages and
disadvantages that each type material offers to designers, builders, revellers, and the economy.

Chemical Reactions
Strand (Topic): Chemical Reactions:
This study allows us to predict how chemicals should react and assist in planning for synthesis of more complex useful products.
Essential Learning Outcome: ELO 1: Analyse and interpret data on the properties of substances before and after the substances interact to
determine if a chemical reaction has occurred.
Clarification Statement: Examples of reactions could include burning sugar or steel wool, fat reacting with sodium hydroxide, and mixing zinc with hydrogen
chloride.
Assessment Boundary: Assessment is limited to analysis of the following properties: density, melting point, boiling point, solubility, flammability, and odour.
Grade Level Expectations: Refer to grade level expectations at the beginning of this curriculum document.

Grade 6 Science Curriculum

After adding the baking soda to the vinegar, prepare to hold a lit
wooden splint at the mouth of the bottle but first ask Learners these
questions:
• Now Learners, we know that to burn something we need
oxygen so if this chemical reaction is producing oxygen, what
will happen? (the splint will continue to burn or burn brighter)
• We also know that carbon dioxide that we breathe out not
only blows out birthday candles but is also used in fire
extinguishers. If this reaction is producing carbon dioxide,
what will likely happen? (It should put the flame out.)
• Lastly, hydrogen gas is very flammable. You may have heard
that hot air balloons of the past that were filled with
hydrogen gas created some big explosions. (see:
https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=fURATK5Yt30 (5:09
mins)
What is likely to happen if the gas produced is hydrogen? (The
splint will burn but maybe explode!)
Proceed to hold the burning splint to the mouth of the bottle- the
splint should go out. Which gas is it Learners? (carbon dioxide)
Explain to Learners that the outcome of this reaction is new materials
we call products of the reaction. Because the balloon sealed off the
bottle, we say that this was a closed system; nothing could escape.
• What did you notice about the masses that you wrote down
before and after the chemical reaction? (they are almost the same)
Why would that be? Let me explain.
Everything in and on Earth is made of matter. The amount of matter
on Earth does not change. Matter is only changed from one form to
another. Matter changes physically and chemically all the time;
however, the total amount of matter always remains the same. This is
known as the Law of Conservation of Matter.
32
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
Matter can neither be created nor destroyed. It can only change from
one substance to another.
In our experiment, there was a chemical change but even though we
have new products, the overall mass of the system did not change.
Experiment 2: Done as a teacher demonstration
Materials required
Two clear plastic cups, Sodium carbonate (Washing soda), Magnesium
sulphate (Epsom salt), Water, Measuring cylinder, Measuring spoon
and Popsicle sticks for stirring
Retrieved from:
https://pixabay.com/photos/popsicle-sticks-sticks-wood-35 0084/
https://www.flickr.com/photos/creative_tools/429793 3700
Demonstration
Place two teaspoons of Epsom salts in a cup and 2 teaspoons of
washing soda in a separate cup. Explain to Learners that these should
not be touched, tasted or smelled as you pass them around to make
observations. They should record their observations in their
notebooks.
33
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
• What do you observe? (a crystalline solid and a powdery solid)
Ask Learners what they think will happen if you add one solid to
another? (a chemical reaction)
Add the Epsom salts to the washing soda and of course nothing
happens. “Learners, I bet you were expecting an explosion!”
In order for a chemical reaction to happen to happen, the reactants
must somehow have collisions, some way of interacting. In this case
the solids can’t interact at the molecular level because they both exist
as solids. They act like marbles added to sand! Nothing happens.
We are going to repeat the combination but this time, I am going to
add 100mL of water to each solid before I combine them. In this way,
the molecules can collide within the medium of water once they are
dissolved.
Show Learners separate containers and the addition of water and
stirring. It looks different now because the solids are now dissolved in
water. The water and the independent solids have not reacted- We
could prove this by evaporating the water off. We would see that we
got the washing powder and Epsom salts right back again- that would
demonstrate only a physical change.
• What do you notice about the two solutions? Did the solids
dissolve? (the solutions are both clear; you can’t see any solids)
• But what do you predict will happen when we combine the
two solutions? (a chemical reaction)
Slowly add the washing soda solution to the Epsom salts solution.
• Learners, what do you see happening? (a solid is forming in the
water and falling to the bottom)
Scientists call that solid a precipitate.
What do you suppose that solid is? It must be some combination of
the starting materials! What did we start with?
34
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
Washing soda has a chemical name sodium carbonate.
Epsom salt has a chemical name magnesium sulfate.
These two chemicals get together and swap partners!
sodium carbonate + magnesium sulfate >
magnesium carbonate + sodium sulfate
As it happens, one product, sodium sulfate readily dissolves in water
but we say that magnesium carbonate precipitates out of the water as
a solid.
So Learners, we see that a precipitate is another indicator of chemical
change.
Compounds of magnesium and calcium are common in our
environment, but they can impact our water supply.
Hard and soft water
Water can be classified as hard water and soft water As water passes
over rocks, some of the minerals (e.g. Calcium and Magnesium), in
these rocks dissolve in the water. As the water picks up the minerals, it
becomes hard water. Hard water has minerals containing magnesium
and calcium salts dissolved in it. Hard water does not lather with soap,
making it difficult to wash clothes with soap.
Soft water on the other hand lathers soap. Rainwater is a very good
example of soft water, as it contains very little dissolved substance
which is why rainwater is suitable for household purposes. As hard
water does not mix well with soap. We have to ‘soften’ hard water
before we can use it
35
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
What happens when soap is mixed with “hard” water?
Soap “scum” is a common example of a precipitate that forms when
certain minerals in water (e.g. calcium and magnesium) react with
soap. In this experiment, you will be making your own hard water
and mixing it with soap to see what happens. You will then compare
the lathering ability of soap and soap scum to find out whether soap
scum is a new substance or it is the same as soap.
Student Experiment
Water, Epsom salt (magnesium sulfate) , liquid soap, paper towels, 2
Popsicle sticks, 2 Empty disposable water bottles (8-oz) with caps,
clear plastic cups, 1 small cup, 1 tablespoon, 1 teaspoon
Procedure
1. Label three plastic cups Soap, Water and Hard Water.
2. Prepare the soap solution (a few drops of liquid soap in a glass
of water and stirring the mixture).
3. Prepare the hard water (2 teaspoons of Epsom salt (magnesium
sulfate) to a glass of water. Stir the solution until no more solid
will dissolve).
4. Place ¼ cup of water in the cup labelled Water.
5. Add 1 tablespoon of the soap solution to both the
Water and Hard Water cups.
6. Look at both cups from the top and the side.
Ask Learners:
● What differences, if any, do you observe in the way soap
combines with water compared with what happened when it
was added to hard water?
36
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
Expected results:
The soap solution will mix with water and cause the water to look
cloudy. When the soap solution is added to hard water, a white curd-
like substance will form. This is the precipitate, “soap scum” which
is the result of magnesium sulfate in the hard water undergoing a
chemical reaction with a soap molecule.
Experiment 3: More Indicators of Chemical Change
Teacher note: This can be done as a teacher demonstration or a
student experiment. The chemical reaction of steel wool (iron) and
vinegar (acetic acid) creates a foul smell so it should be carried out in a
well-ventilated area inside the classroom or outside.
Materials needed: Vinegar, steel wool, thermometer, Ziplock® bag,
graduated cylinder, glass reaction container (bowl or beaker)
Procedure
Place the steel wool in a Ziplock® plastic bag and pass it around the
classroom for Learners to observe and take note of the characteristics
of the steel wool. Explain to them that this is iron wool (symbol Fe).
Place 20 mL of vinegar in a bowl or beaker for each student group and
ask them to observe and take note of the characteristics of the vinegar
(e.g. clear liquid with sharp acrid smell) Teacher note: Again, remind
Learners of the practice of wafting a smell towards their nose rather
than putting their nose in close proximity to the vinegar.
(https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=m0xoin9snHQ)
Within the bag, have Learners break off a piece of the steel wool,
remove it from the bag and place it in the reaction bowl containing the
20mL of vinegar.
Be sure to cover the steel wool with vinegar and let it set for one minute.
37
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
Remove the steel wool from the vinegar and gently squeeze out any
excess vinegar.
Wrap the steel wool around the base of the thermometer and place the
thermometer into the clean glass beaker. Cover the beaker with a paper
towel or a lid to trap the heat inside.
Record the starting temperature and then record the temperature every
30 seconds for 5 minutes
Ask them to record their observations in their notebooks.
Sample notebook:
Observations of starting materials
Steel wool:
Vinegar:
Temperature (degrees C) Time (secs)
0
30
60 > 5 mins
Observations of reaction and products:
In their notebooks, Learners should plot a graph from their data table
where temperature is on the y axis (ordinate) and time is on the x axis
(abscissa). Have Learners comment on the shape of the graph (increase
in temperature and then leveling of temperature as reaction subsides)
What signs did they see that a chemical reaction maybe happening?
• Disappearance of steel wool starting material
38
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
• Color change (brownish orange)
• Temperature change (increases and then levels off)
• New odour given off
➢ Learners what do you think the products may have been?
(Perhaps, something with iron in it?)
➢ What was the color change did you see? (orange brown)
➢ Did it remind you of anything you may have seen in the
community that was made of iron? (rusty nails, rusty bicycle, rusty
car, rusty railing)
Rust is iron reacted with oxygen in the air. (Fe O )
2 3
Let me explain this reaction you have just seen.
Explanation:
Steel wool contains iron. It has a protective coating that keeps
oxygen from coming into contact with the iron. When the steel wool
is soaked in the vinegar, the vinegar which contains acetic acid,
removes this coating. When the protective coating is removed, the
iron is exposed and oxygen in the atmosphere can reach the iron in
the steel wool and reacts with it to form rust, also called iron oxide
The iron and the oxygen are referred to as reactants while the rust or
oxide, is the product of the reaction.
The reaction of iron and oxygen creates heat and therefore we call
it an exothermic reaction
Real world applications:
1. Why do you think some items made from steel will

sometimes rust? (scratching the paint off a car or bicycle)

3. Ask Learners to make a list of items in the community that

4. What do you think can be done to help to prevent
rusting? (Coating item with a layer of paint of grease)
5. Why do you think certain items are made from stainless
steel instead of iron? (Stainless steel less likely to rust)
6. Find the cost of an item that is made from stainless steel
and one made from iron? (stainless steel is more costly)
One Last Indicator of Chemical Change: Light
Burning of a candle or wood is the heating of the reactant to a high
enough temperature in the presence of oxygen. This causes what
we call burning and burning gives off light. Besides carbon dioxide
and water, combustion (burning) produces light!
Other things can burn in the presence of oxygen and produce large
bright amounts of light. A good example is the burning of
magnesium wire. See here:
https://www.youtube.com/shorts/1HCtPCHFA5Q
In fact, burning magnesium wire in a closed system was often used
for flash lighting in old cameras. (see:
https://www.youtube.com/shorts/5eUIepuZjKc
40
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
Light is also given off in complex biochemical reactions within the
bodies of animals. This is called bioluminescence.
(see: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=fYsAK24le6U (3:29
mins) Examples include fireflies and Deep sea animals

etc. Medicines that we use when we are sick, such as cold medicines, are prepared by chemists in the laboratory by performing chemical reactions. Attempt
to make as many connections as possible to real world examples.

Elements from Local Culture, Technology, TVET, Environment that are integrated:
Culture
● Many chemical reactions are involved in cultural activities (burning candles, burning wood to roast breadfruit, roasting breadfruit, corn, etc. for
campfire, cooking etc., burning sugar for pelau and toolum (type of sugar cakes.)
● As many of the islands have underground springs, the Learners can be asked to compare the ability of detergents to lather in rainwater, tap wate
and spring water.
● Many chemicals are used in everyday life. For example, medicines, cleaning agents, etc., glow sticks in crafts and costumes.
● Fireworks used in festive occasions
Environment
● After rainfall mud sometimes mix with water in the river and affect our water system/reservoir
● Hard water resulting from chemicals dissolving in the water as it passes over rocks or drains through soils
Technology
● Many of the materials that are involved in technological devices, e.g. computers, phones, etc. are created from chemical reactions.
● Many technologies are used to determine if new products are formed and to study these products.
TVET
● A number of areas in TVET utilise both physical changes (e.g. sanding and painting) and chemical changes (hair dressing, dyeing, etc.) in their activities.
Agriculture
● Use of chemicals in agriculture can affect organisms in the environment

Reviewing a Past Reaction
You may remember that we can rinse steel wool with vinegar
and then expose it to oxygen in the air and it will form iron
oxide (rust) in a chemical reaction.
If we place the rinsed steel wool in a flask and cover the flask
with a balloon two interesting things happen:

Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies

(2:26 mins) (entire experiment shown here)
1. The balloon will be pushed into the flask as the iron
consumes the oxygen and makes iron oxide (rust),
2. The mass of the system, start to finish, does not
change.
Because this is a closed system, this experiment once again
demonstrates that the mass of the reactants and products does
not change. We know this is because atoms are redistributed
but neither lost or created within the chemical reaction
Considering Conservation at the Atomic Level
Methane (CH ) is a gas made up of carbon and hydrogen.
4
Methane is emitted during the production and transport of coal,
natural gas, and oil. Methane emissions also result from
livestock and other agricultural practices and by the decay of
organic waste in municipal solid waste landfills.

This is called a balanced combustion reaction because it

Ask Learners to look carefully at the chemical structures of each

• Ask them to count the black colored balls for carbon
(C) on each side of the reaction (1 black ball reactant side,
1 black ball product side)
• Ask them to count the white colored balls for hydrogen
(H) on each side of the reaction (4 white balls reactant
side, 4 white balls product side)
• Ask them to count the red colored balls for oxygen (O)
on each side of the reaction (4 red balls reactant side, 4 red
balls product side)

Explain to Learners that this is an example of conservation of
matter that involves a chemical reaction
Introduce a Review Example- Copper Pipe
Give Learners the scenario of a copper pipe that has been
weighed.
• Ask them what happens to the pipe if left in the open
air? (it will rust)
When metals like iron and copper combine with oxygen in the
open air, we say they become oxidized to form rust.
When the copper grabs oxygen from the air, matter has not
increased overall because the copper (Cu) simply reacted with
the oxygen in the atmosphere that itself has mass. This may be
harder to understand because this is an open system but
overall the mass in the entire system has not changed.
50
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
Retrieved from:
https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=VvbX8PitSpg (5:52 mins)
Point out to Learners that in this oxidation reaction, the
number of atoms of each component on the reactant and
product side is the same. The air gave up some oxygen to
oxidized (rust) the copper pipe but overall, the amount of
matter in the world has not changed, it has just been
reorganized.
Representing a Chemical Reaction
In much of our discussions to date, we have written word
equations to represent chemical reactions. However, these
word equations do not tell us what atoms or molecules are
actually involved in the reaction. To describe chemical
reactions, scientists use balanced chemical equations, which
make use of symbols and formulas for elements, molecules
and compounds. In a chemical equation as with word
equations, the reactants are shown on the left-hand side of the
equation and the products are shown on the right-hand side
of the equation.
For example, the balanced chemical equation for the reaction of
sodium and water is the following:
51
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
From this equation, we can see that on the left-hand side we
have sodium and water as reactants. The 2 in front of sodium
tells us there are two sodium atoms reacting and the 2 in front
of water tell us there are 2 molecules of water reacting. These
numbers are referred to as coefficients in the balanced
equation.
Teacher note: reiterate that atoms come together to make
molecules.
Learners,
• How many molecules of sodium hydroxide are
produced? (2)
• How many molecules of hydrogen gas are produced (1)
*explain to Learners that we sometimes don’t add a 1
coefficient because 1 molecule is implied *
If you look again at the equation, you will see the number 2
after the H in water on the reactant side and 2 after the H on
the product side. These numbers called subscripts are simply
the number of atoms that make up the molecule. Example:
There are 2 atoms of H in every water molecule and 2 atoms of
H in every hydrogen gas molecule (H )
2

Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
Let us do an atom count on each side of the reaction to confirm
conservation of matter.

Opportunities for Subject Integration:
Mathematics
• Use tools to measure volume, temperature, mass, etc.
• Count the number of atoms (reactants and products) in a chemical reaction.
• Apply mathematical operations to understanding chemical equations.
• Tabulate data collected on chemical reactions.
• Calculate the mass of reactants or products.
Language Arts:
• Describe substances before and after chemical reactions
• Learners can write descriptive pieces to record their observations.
• Introduction to new vocabulary such as: conservation, chemical equation, balance, etc.
Social Studies:
53
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
• There is need for sustainability in the environment, use of resources to conduct chemical reactions, reduction of pollution from these reactions
Art and craft
• Make physical models to represent chemical reactions
• Draw models of atoms and molecules to show how reactions proceed from reactants to products
Elements from Local Culture, Technology, TVET, Environment that are integrated:
Elements from Local Culture
• The use of scales, measuring cups, and measuring spoons in domestic kitchens, restaurants, and guest houses
• The use of measuring instruments to administer the correct dosages of medicines
• Making local coconut oils, hair products, candles, and confectioneries.
Environment
• Burning wood, old tyres, etc. release a large quantity of substances into the atmosphere, where many are not aware that these substances do not disappear; they
only change form.
• Chemicals that get released into the air do not disappear. They go into our atmosphere where they react with other substances.
• Law of Conservation of matter/mass may be linked to sustainability, the effects of various forms of matter which may change from one form to another
and impact the environment in the process (for example solid/liquid chemicals that change into gases that pollute the environment.
Technology·
• Using a scale to measure luggage at airports to ensure that correct weight allowance is maintained
• Use of 3D printers to change the shape and form of matter
TVET
• The materials to make many TVET tools are as a result of chemical reactions.
• Chemical reactions are used extensively to identify and analyze materials
Agriculture
• Conservation of mass is seen in the uptake of substances which are converted to mass of the plant.
• Photosynthesis is a reaction that demonstrates conservation of mass.

Understanding the fundamentals of a balanced equation is worth spending significant time on with Learners as it serves as a foundation for practical planning
for more formal experiments later.

Inheritance, Variation of Traits and Life Cycles
Strand (Topic): Inheritance, Variation of Traits and Life Cycles: (expanded from Grade 3).
Studies of inheritance allow us to predict both desirable and undesirable traits and diseases that could be passed between generations.
Essential Learning Outcome: ELO 1: Develop models to describe that organisms have unique and diverse life cycles but all have in common birth,
growth, reproduction, and death
Clarification Statement: Changes organisms go through during their life form a pattern.
Assessment Boundary: Assessment of plant life cycles is limited to those of flowering plants. Assessment does not include details of human reproduction.
Grade Level Expectations: Refer to grade level expectations at the beginning of this curriculum document.

In the diagram below, explain what is The seed
happening at the stages labelled A and B.
A seed is a structure that contains a small embryonic plant covered by a
thin outer covering called the seed coat. The embryo is what will grow to
become a fully-grown plant. Seeds come in all different shapes and sizes,
from the tiny orchid seed to the palm tree seed, to the large seed of a
coconut.
Teacher note: Review the different parts of the seed with Learners and
their functions. Experiments on germination can also be set up for
Learners’ observation, if necessary.
Label the following diagram of a seed.
Retrieved from: https://www.sciencefacts.net/parts-of-a-seed.html
https://www.education.com/worksheet/article/parts-of-a-seed-1/
Germination
Once a seed is provided with the necessary resources and receives the right
signals for germination, the seed will begin to absorb water through its
seed coat, which will expand and burst open. The embryo found inside
the seed, is made up of a shoot and a root. The root emerges first from
inside the seed and begins to grow down into the soil. Next, the shoot
emerges and grows upwards towards the sun. The shoot develops into a
small seedling.

plant grows flowers, fruits and leaves.

Drawings and pictures (3 marks) Pollinators
Punctuation and grammar (5 marks) Since plants cannot move from place to place, most of them need help
with pollination. Animals that pollinate flowers are called pollinators.
Pollinators include bees, butterflies, moths, wasps, flies, bats, birds and
even hummingbirds but the most common pollinators are insects.
Retrieved from: https://pixabay.com/photos/bee-honey-bee-insect-fauna-
bloom-7413333/
https://pixabay.com/photos/insect-lime-butterfly-entomology-7025881/
https://pixabay.com/photos/rufus-hummingbird-bird-humming-4885971
How pollinators move pollen?
When creatures come to feed or collect sweet nectar (made at the base of
the petals), from the flowers, or shelter and nest-building materials, they
become covered in pollen. As they fly from flower to flower in order to
collect nectar, they accidentally rub against the stamens and get pollen stuck
all over themselves. When they move to another flower to feed, some of
the pollen can rub off onto this new plant's stigma.
77
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
Retrieved from:
https://www.britannica.com/science/cross-pollination
People can accidentally transfer pollen from one flower to another by going
from flower to flower and handling them. Humans can also manually
pollinate some flowers
Discuss why farmers sometimes have to transfer pollen for themselves.
Similarly, when animals pass by a flower, pollen can be carried on their
bodies and fur and transferred to different flowers.
On a breezy day, pollen can be transported by air currents. Plants that are
wind-pollinated include wheat, rice, corn and rye. Pollen can also be
transferred from plant to plant by water.
Features of Insect pollinated plants
• Flowers are brightly coloured, scented and secrete nectar.
• Pollen grains are larger, sticky and spiny.
• Pollen grains are fewer in number
• The stigma of flowers is small and deep within the flower.
• The stamens are within the flower tube
78
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
• Some may have a shape that fits with the pollinator (i.e. hibiscus
and hummingbird).
Retrieved from:
https://pixabay.com/photos/tomato-blossom-tomatoes-blossom-
1447877/ https://pixabay.com/photos/flower-pumpkin-yellow-
nature-yard-5394594/ https://pixabay.com/photos/hibiscus-red-
hibiscus-red-flower-1052018/
Features of Wind pollinated plants
• Flowers have no bright colours, special odours, or nectar.
• Flowers are small and most have no petals.
• Stamens and stigmas are exposed to air currents.
• Large amounts of pollen are produced.
• Pollen grains are smooth, light and easily airborne.
• Stigma are feathery to catch pollen from the wind.
Examples of wind-pollinated plants include: wheat, rice, corn, rye, barley,
several grasses and oats.

Observing pollinators
For the first part of this activity, take Learners around the neighbourhood
for a casual observation walk. If a flower garden is nearby, it can also be
used.
Before Learners are taken on the visit/walk, visit the area to see the plants
with brightly coloured flowers, where they are located and the pollinators
that are found in the area. Before you leave for the visit/walk, remind
Learners that they should not remove or damage any of the plants.
When they get to the area, ask Learners to do the following:
1. Make a list of the plants with brightly coloured flowers. Take as
many pictures of the pictures of the flowers as possible. Make
sketches of these flowers if you do not have a camera. Note their
colour and size.
2. Make a note of the insects or birds that visit these flowers. Find out
the names of these organisms with your teacher’s help.
3. Try to catch a few of the insect pollinators, using a net, not your
hands. See if a yellow powdery substance is sticking to their legs and
bodies. This substance is pollen (pollen grains).
4. Take as many pictures as possible of the organisms visiting the
flowers.
5. When Learners return to the classroom, discuss their findings with
them. Make a list of the pollinators that were seen and the flowers
they visited.
After the class discussion:
1. Assign each group a pollinator. Provide the group with a Profile card
that corresponds to the pollinator that they were assigned.
2. Pass out the Pollinator Observation Data Sheet.
3. Learners will then return to the area they first visited and use the data
sheet on their pollinator to find out the following:
80
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
4. What number of flowers did your pollinator visit the most? (Answers
will vary.)
5. What are three flower traits that you think attracted your pollinator?
(Answers will vary.)
Other questions
1. Why do pollinators visit flowers? (Pollinators visit flowers in search of
nectar and pollen, to use as food).
2. How do pollinators know which flowers to go to?
(There are many different ways pollinators decide which flowers to visit, these
include the colour, taste, patterns and popularity of the flower.)
3. What are two strategies that flowers have to attract the most
pollinators? (Many flowers use colours to attract insects, sometimes helped by
coloured marks to guide them. Flowers are often shaped to provide a landing
platform for visiting insects to land on or to force them to brush
against anthers and stigmas.)
What is fertilization
After a flower is pollinated, each pollen grain which is on the stigma grows
a tiny tube down towards the ovules in the ovary.
81
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
Retrieved from: https://k8schoollessons.com/pollination-and-
fertilization/
Then the male gametes in pollen grains can reach the ovules, the pollen
tube breaks into the ovule and join female gametes or eggs produced in
ovules. The female gametes or eggs then join with the male gametes. This
joining or the combination of male gametes and female gametes is called
fertilization. Fertilization cannot take place without pollination.
After fertilization, a seed forms. The fertilized egg grows to become the
embryo found in a seed that will later grow into a new plant. There is also a
protective coat around the seed called the seed coat.
The petals, the stamen and the stigma then fall off and the ovary develops
into a fruit to protect the seed; fruits take on a variety of different shapes
and sizes and colour.
Fruits contain one, a few or many seeds. Some flowers, such as avocados,
have only one ovule in their ovary, so their fruit will have only one seed.
Many flowers, such as kiwifruit, have lots of ovules in their ovaries, so their
fruit contains many seeds. The fruit also helps in dispersing the seeds from
the parent plant to continue the cycle.
82
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
Questions:
1. Why do you think the petals, the stamen and the stigma fall off?
(They fall off because they are no longer needed.)
2. Why is it important to protect the embryo?
(It is necessary to protect the embryo from drying out and from any injuries. If
the embryo is damaged, it will not be able to germinate and produce a new
plant.)
3. Give 2 examples of fruits that contain 1 seed, a few seed and many
seeds
• (Examples of fruits that contain one seed are: Avocado pear,
Coconut, Mango, Plum.
• Examples of fruits that contain a few seeds are:
Sapodilla, Grape, Apple, Grapefruit
• Examples of fruits that contain many seeds are:
Watermelon, Papaya, Guava, Passion Fruit Dragon fruit,
Pomegranate)
Activity: Fertilization
Dissect the flower and show Learners the path the pollen takes.
1. Have Learners trace the path of the pollen and label where
fertilization happens on their diagrams.
2. Observe the stamen (with anthers and filament) and the small,
thin, pistil. Do you see pollen? (Yes/No)
3. There is an anther cap; pollen is not released until the flower is
ready. How is this pistil different from the diagrams you have
seen? (Answers will depend on the type of flower used).
4. How many stamens are there? (Answers will depend on the type of flower
used).
5. Draw the stamens and pistils in your notebook and label them.
83
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
6. Gently pull out each of the stamens. Only a pistil and ovary (the
bottom bulb-like structure) will remain.
Dissect the ovary.
1. With your toothpick, make a vertical slice down the middle of the
ovary and open it like a book.
2. Place the two halves of the ovary on your plate. What do you see
inside the ovary? What do you notice about the way ovules are
arranged? What needs to happen so ovules grow into seeds? Draw
your dissected ovary with ovules in your notebook and label them.
Use your hand lens to see the arrangement of ovules. Select a few
on the end of your toothpick to observe.
3. Indicate the location of pollen grains and ovules on a diagram of a
flower and trace the path from the stigma to the ovary of a flower.
Seed dispersal
The final stage in a flowering plant life cycle is seed dispersal. These
seeds, when ready, are dispersed into the environment, so that they can
themselves germinate and grow into new plants. The seeds are dispersed by
wind, animals, and water.
Teacher note
Review with Learners the agents of dispersal and discuss the role that
dispersal plays in the life cycle of plants.
Plant death
Eventually the plant will age and die. This is the end of the life cycle for
that individual, not the end of the species. This may not happen until the
plant has reproduced several times. If the plant had successfully produced
seeds, its species would survive into the future as the cycle begins again.
84
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
Discuss with Learners why plant death is an important part of the plant life
cycle.

Waves and Electromagnetic Radiation
Strand (Topic): Waves and Electromagnetic Radiation:
An understanding of the properties of waves helps Learners to account for natural phenomena including the behaviour of light and further establishes the
foundation for explaining interactions of matter based on an atomic theory.

wave is related to the energy in a wave.
Clarification Statement: Emphasis is on describing waves with both qualitative and quantitative thinking.
Assessment Boundary: Assessment does not include electromagnetic waves and is limited to standard repeating waves.
Grade Level Expectations: Refer to grade level expectations at the beginning of this curriculum document.

Wave: A ripple/vibration that moves through a medium or
space. It carries energy from one place to another
Wave amplitude: This is how high or low the wave goes
from its resting position. It is the distance from the middle of
the wave to the top (crest) or bottom (trough)
Crest: The highest point on a transverse wave.
Trough: The lowest point on a transverse wave.
Frequency: How many waves go past a point in one
Distinguishing High & Low Frequency Waves second? Measured in hertz (Hz).

wave and a low frequency wave to demonstrate
they know the difference.

Mathematicians have determined that there is a
relationship between the amplitude of the wave and
the energy it carries.
The amount of energy carried by a wave is related
to the amplitude of the wave. A high-energy wave
is characterized by a high amplitude; a low-energy
wave is characterized by a low amplitude.
The energy of the wave is directly proportional to
the square of the amplitude. (E α A2)
Amplitude (meters) Energy (joules)
proportional to

Water Waves and Energy: Activity
Materials: Bowl of water, assorted pebbles

experiments and draw conclusions:
1) Drop a large pebble and a small pebble
from the same height. What do you notice
about the waves created? (Large pebble higher
waves). What does this mean about the
energy of the falling pebble? (the larger pebble
96
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
has more energy and that is reflected in the
amplitude of the wave on impact with the water)
2) Drop the larger pebble from two different
heights (.5 meters and 1 metre). What do
you notice about the waves (higher drop
height causes bigger waves)? Which of the two
heights likely involves more energy? (the
higher pebble makes the bigger wave; I think it has
more energy)
Using Waves to Transmit Sound: A Simple
Phone We Can Build
Learners can design and build a simple amplifier
and state how the amplitude affects the quality of
the sound produced.
Materials:
● Two Styrofoam® cups
● A piece of string
● A pencil/pen
● Scissors
● Tape/glue
Instructions for making sound amplifier (as shown
in the picture below)
● Use a pencil to poke a small hole in the
center of the bottom of a Styrofoam® cup
● Cut a string to a length of about 1 meter
● Thread one end of the string through the
hole in the cup. Tie a knot on the inside of
the cup so that the string doesn’t fall back
out through the hole.
97
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
● Take a second cup and make a similar hole
through the center at the bottom.
● Pass the other end of the string through
the hole in the second cup and tie a knot to
secure it.
Retrieved from:
https://raisinglifelonglearners.com/wp-
content/uploads/2017/03/string-telephone-
explanation-5.jpg
● Hold the cups with the strings pulled taut
between them
● Have a student speak into one cup while
the other listens.
● Ask Learners to account for their
observations. Why does this apparatus act
as an amplifier? (When you speak into the cup,
your voice creates waves that travel through the
string to the second cup. The cups help the sound
waves to spread out and get louder when they reach
your ear.)
98
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
● The first student will speak into the cup
with a whisper, then a speaking voice and
then a shout. A second student will note
observations.
● Discussion: Learners will explain what their
observations tell about the relationship
between amplitude and volume (Speaking
louder into the first cup increases the amplitude of
the sound waves produced. The louder sound heard
in the second cup is due to higher energy carried by
the sound waves. When the speaker speaks louder
the amplitude of the sound waves increases and the
energy transmitted through the string also increases)

Sound Waves Travel Through Air
Show Learners a drum with pieces of sand on the
skin. Strike the drum. Ask Learners if they can hear
it. (yes) Can you see the sand bouncing? (yes). That is
because the drum sound results from the drum
skin's vibration. If we could see the sound leaving
the drum it would appear as a wave. But how do we
hear that drum? (with our ears) But how does that
happen? (not sure)
Our ear also has a drum -called an eardrum. When
the waves enter the ear, they vibrate the eardrum,
and that signal goes to our brain. Some waves come
rapidly, other waves come slowly; this allows our
ear to distinguish different types of sound, for
example, high pitch versus low pitch.

https://people.emich.edu/blow/howto.html

(Completed worksheet) 1) Reflection when the wave bounces off the
barrier and moves off in the opposite direction.
2) Transmission when the wave passes through
the substance and continues moving in the same
direction. This occurs when the barrier allows the
wave to pass without obstructing or absorbing it
e.g. light waves through clear glass.
3) Absorption is when the barrier absorbs the
wave's energy, reducing its (the wave’s) intensity.
The absorbed energy is converted into another form
of energy such as heat e.g. sunlight being absorbed
by a dark colored bottle
Reinforcing the Concepts
Dim the lights in the classroom and use a flashlight
to shine a light on different objects e.g. a mirror,
transparent plastic, and black paper. Learners will
explain their observations. They will also explain
how these activities demonstrate reflection,
absorption, and transmission of light.
Observations:
1. Mirror-Bright flashlight beam and their reflections
are observed. This is because the mirror has a
smooth, shiny surface which causes light to be
bounced back or be reflected.
2. Transparent plastic-Objects behind the plastic
become illuminated and can be seen. This is
because the plastic is transparent, allowing most
light to pass through or be transmitted.
3. Black paper-The paper appears dark and nothing
behind it can be seen. After a while the paper may
feel warm This is because the dark surface absorbs

most of the light, converting it to heat, and very
little is reflected to the observer
How Can We Use the Reflection Property?
Ask Learners if they have ever heard of a periscope.
Periscopes are used in submarines so that you can
see far above the surface of the water without the
submarine being above the water line. This is in
part a defense against ships that might see the
submarine in wartime and attack.

pattern of light and dark areas on the
wall. Describe the interactions
occurring between the light waves and
the window.
The pattern of light and dark areas is caused by
transmission and shadow formation.
The parts of the window that allow light to pass
through create bright areas on the wall, while the
frames or dividers block the light, creating shadows
or dark areas. The light waves that pass through
the glass are transmitted, while the parts of the
window that do not allow light through create the
pattern of light and dark areas on the wall. Sunlight bounces off the mountain and travels to the lake.
Some of the light bounces off (reflects off) the lake, like light
reflecting off a mirror. The reflected light travels to your eye

s (0.20 mins)
When the ocean meets the shore or encounters a
barrier the “water wave” gets reflected and bounces
off in another direction to which it approaches the
structure. This may cause chaotic or irregular wave
patterns. Next time you visit the beach look out for
reflection and notice the pattern in the water at the
shoreline.

Can Sound Waves be Reflected?
Set up the apparatus below:
If we take two tubes and position them against a
wall, as shown in the figure above, upon placing a
speaker or any other source of sound near one end
of the tube, we receive the sound at the end of the
other tube. This activity proves that the surface of
wall reflects the sound wave.

Absorption of Sunlight Waves
Ask Learners if they have ever noticed a difference
in the inside temperature of cars on hot days?
As an experiment, paint one 2L plastic pop bottle
with white paint and one 2L bottle with black paint.
After the painted bottles have dried, fill them both
with water. Place the two bottles in direct sunlight
for 2 hours. Use a thermometer to take the
temperature of the two bottles. Ask Learners which
bottle is the warmest. (They will find the black bottle is
warmest) Ask them why it is the warmest. (The black
color absorbs sun rays while the white bottle reflects them
therefore the water in the black bottle will be warmer.)
Ask Learners: In the absence of air conditioning,
which color car will be the cooler car in the hot
sun? (white car will reflect sunlight and stay cool; black car
will get hot inside)

Many windows in a house will allow light to pass
freely and therefore we can easily see through them.
These are said to be transparent (The top part of
the window). Glass and some plastic may be
transparent
Some windows are said to be translucent because
they diffuse (reflect in different directions) most of
the light. Materials like frosted glass and some
plastics are called translucent. They only a small
amount of light pass through so that you don’t see
easily through the window (the bottom part of the
window).
What is different with this window?

Opaque objects like the window above block light
from traveling through them. Most of the light is
either reflected by the object or absorbed and
converted to thermal energy. Materials such as
wood, stone, and metals are opaque to visible light.

What does sound need in order to be
transferred as a wave?
We have already seen that sound readily transfers
through air (to our ears) and water ((whale talk- see:
https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=WabT1L-nN-
E (2:57 mins))
Have Learners watch the following video and
explain why they can’t hear the sound?
https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=jMEgzsmJdm
Q&feature=youtu.be (1:01 mins)
(When air is removed from a system to generate a vacuum,
the sound wave has no wave medium in which to travel
so we don’t hear anything.)
What would you predict would happen if you
pass a light through a vacuum chamber? (it
would shine through)
In fact, light will easily pass through a vacuum. This
is because light sometimes acts like a wave but also
acts as a particle and with nothing in its way it can
pass through the vacuum. Think of the analogy of a
spaceship as a discrete particle. Once it is given
enough velocity to leave the Earth, it readily passes
through space which is the biggest vacuum!
Have Learners watch the following video and
then explain why sound travels faster in water
compared with air.

0 (6:22 mins)
An explanation: the relay race
Sound travels by particles bumping into each other
as they vibrate. It is a little like a relay race – each
runner holds a little bit of information (the baton),
and when they make contact with the next runner,
they pass the information on.
In the case of sound, the runners are particles and
the information (baton) they are passing along is
energy of vibration. In a sound wave, a particle
picks up some energy and keeps it until it bumps
into a neighbouring particle. The next particle will
then pick up the energy and transfer it to the next
one in the chain. This happens extremely fast and is
detected as a wave of pressure.
Sound won’t travel in a vacuum because there are
no particles to bump together to transmit the
vibration.
Quoted verbatim from:
https://www.sciencelearn.org.nz/resources/572-
sound-on-the-move

Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Forces and Interactions
Strand (Topic): Forces and Interactions
Force and motion are important parts of everyday life. As Learners study this unit, they will learn how these physical factors impact their lives and work. The
lessons and activities will help Learners become aware of factors like friction, gravity, and magnetic force.
Essential Learning Outcome: ELO 1: Apply Newton’s Third Law to design a solution to a problem involving the motion of two colliding objects.
Clarification Statement: Examples of practical problems could include the impact of collisions between two cars, between a car and stationary objects, and
between a meteor and a space vehicle.
Assessment Boundary: Assessment is limited to vertical or horizontal interactions in one dimension.
Grade Level Expectations: Refer to grade level expectations at the beginning of this curriculum document.

1) A bird stays airborne by flapping their wings
Retrieved from:
https://stock.adobe.com/search?k=%22bird+flapping+wings
%22
A bird flies through the air by flapping its wings and pushing
the air downwards.
According to Newton’s third law, for every action, there is an
equal and opposite reaction. Do you think the air has an equal
force acting on the bird? In what direction is this force acting?
(The size of the force on the air equals the size of the force on the bird; the
direction of the force on the air (downwards) is opposite the direction of the
force on the bird (upwards))

Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
2) While driving down the road, a firefly strikes the
windshield of a bus and makes an obvious mess in
front of the face of the driver
Retrieved from: https://slideplayer.com/slide/15063990/
While driving down the road, a firefly strikes the windshield of
a bus and makes a quite obvious mess in front of the face of
the driver. This is a clear case of Newton's third law of
motion. The firefly hit the bus and the bus hit the firefly.
Which of the two forces is greater: the force on the firefly or
the force on the bus? (Each force is the same size.)
Newton’s 3d Law of Motion and Space
Retrieved from: https://edu.gcfglobal.org/en/newtons-laws-
of-motion/newtons-third-law/1/
Discussion
Newton’s third law states that for every action, there is an
equal and opposite reaction. In space there is no gravity so
astronauts tend to float around in the spaceship.
129
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
1) Do you think the 3rd law applies to astronauts in
space?
Yes it does, astronauts struggle with the law because there is no gravity in
space and thus, their every action has greater opposite reactions. For
example, if an astronaut pushes on a surface they are propelled in the
opposite direction. In outer space, astronauts use backpack jet packs that
expel a gas. Without friction the astronaut moves easily in the opposite
direction.
2) Simple tasks on earth such as moving boxes and using
a screwdriver are much harder for an astronaut in
space where there is no gravity. What do you think
would happen to an astronaut trying to use a
screwdriver in space?
(due to the lack of gravity and Newton’s third law, the astronaut would
find himself turning or twisting if he is not anchored to a wall)
3) Describe what you think would happen to an
astronaut freely floating in space and pushing a small
box away from himself.
(Despite having different masses, the astronaut and the box would both
apply an equal and opposite force onto each other and move in opposite
directions. Since there is no gravity and no ground to push against; both
objects would simply float away. The box would move much faster since it
has a smaller mass). The picture below shows what is likely to
happen in terms of forces.
130
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
Retrieved from:
https://link.springer.com/referenceworkentry/10.1007/978-
3-319-55065-7_1081
Let's visualise this space concept using a video:
https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ZkVU-bj9bDk (3:07
mins) Think about the following questions as you watch.
1. What did you observe once the astronaut applied a
force to the basketball in microgravity? (due to the
difference in masses, the basketball accelerated much faster than
the astronaut although an equal and opposite force was applied)
2. What did you observe once the astronaut applied a
force to an astronaut of a similar mass? (both astronauts
accelerated in opposite directions)

131
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=a-wh3fJRdjo (4:25
mins)
1. State Newton’s third law (for every action, there is an equal
and opposite reaction)
2. Describe the action and reaction forces of a rocket
being launched (the rocket exhaust moves downwards while
the rocket moves upwards)
3. Describe the action and reaction forces of a cannon
being fired (the cannon's backward movement is the reaction
to the action of shooting a cannonball)
4. What did Newton observe about the magnitude of
action and reaction forces? (action and reaction forces are
always equal)
5. What is the magnitude of the reaction force if a skater
pushes off the ground with 15 Newtons of force? (the
ground pushes back with a reaction force of 15N)
6. Describe the effect of friction on a moving body
(frictional forces would move in the opposite direction of the
moving body causing more energy to be used)
7. Describe three examples of action and reaction forces
which you may encounter in your everyday life.

Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Essential Learning Outcome: ELO 2: Plan an investigation to provide evidence that the change in an object’s motion depends on the sum of the
forces on the object and the mass of the object.
Clarification Statement: Emphasis is on balanced (Newton’s First Law) and unbalanced forces in a system, qualitative comparisons of forces, mass and
changes in motion (Newton’s Second Law), frame of reference, and specification of units.
Assessment Boundary: Assessment is limited to forces and changes in motion in one-dimension in an inertial reference frame and to change in one variable at
a time. Assessment does not include the use of trigonometry.
Grade Level Expectations: Refer to grade level expectations at the beginning of this curriculum document.

Billiards
Ask Learners to use their simulation to explain
what should happen in the following scenario.
When a blue billiard ball is shot directly at a
stationary red ball, what should happen to both
balls?
The red ball moves on impact at the same speed as the
incoming blue ball and the blue ball stops. The balls are both
the same mass, so the momentum of the incoming blue ball is
transferred to the red ball and it travels in the same direction.

Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
Retrieved from: https://orlandoautobody.com/car-
accidents/statistics-and-costs-of-a-car-accident/
Explain that the car engine provides the force for
the car to move. When the car collides with another
car with its own force that the motion that happens
on impact depends on the initial forces of the cars.
It is important to note that there are two types of
collisions: An elastic, or 'bouncy' collision is one
where kinetic energy is conserved, KE is the same
before and after a collision- the objects bounce off
each other like billiard balls colliding By contrast, in
an inelastic, or 'sticky' collision, kinetic energy is
NOT conserved; instead some energy is absorbed
by the surroundings (example bending metal, heat).
In a perfectly inelastic collision both objects stick
together and move forward as one.
Retrieved, in part from:
https://study.com/learn/lesson/elastic-vs-
inelastic-collision-theories-examples.html
NOTE: There are examples of collisions where the
colliding objects don’t stick together but are still
considered inelastic.
Simulating an Inelastic Collision in the
Classroom
After they have shared their prior knowledge, ask
them to think about what factors might affect
where the vehicles might end up after a head on
collision (i.e. relative to where they impacted).
(speed of vehicles, mass of vehicles, direction of vehicles)

Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
Explain that when we talk about an object of mass
M moving at a velocity V we say it has
momentum. If a heavy ball is thrown to you to
catch, it has momentum which you feel when you
catch it as a result of the velocity it is traveling and
the mass that it has.
The teacher will clear off a large smooth desk at the
front of the classroom. The teacher will choose in
advance a heavy textbook and 2 noticeably lighter
textbooks. For best results, the teacher should place
two-sided tape or Velcro® on the spine of the
books.
Pairs of Learners will come to the front of the class
and stand at opposite ends of the table each taking
one of the books. The teacher will instruct the
Learners that each of these books represents a
vehicle. They will then each give a single push of
the books across the table directly at each other
with the book spines pointed at each other, so they
stick together on contact. Learners in the class will
observe the results.
The key focus question is where do joined books
end up relative to the impact location and why?
This simulation will be used to help Learners
answer questions about the following scenario.

Elastic Collisions
You have perhaps seen Newton’s cradle.
Retrieved from:
https://science.howstuffworks.com/innovation/in
ventions/newtons-cradle.htm
The energy of the collision is passed on to the next
ball as they swing downward. However, the loss of
energy to heat (friction) eventually brings the balls
to a stop so this is not a purely elastic collision.

Testing The Electromagnet
Each student group will make an electromagnet with the
available materials.
Learners are expected to create a formal report from the
findings of their electromagnet experiment. It should include a
diagram (5 marks, experimental data (5 marks),
graph (5 marks) and
answers to experimental questions (5 marks) (see below)
NOTE: To begin, the teacher can assist them to create an
electromagnet with 10 turns of wire on their nail.
Ask the Learners to configure their electromagnet
so the number of turns of wire around the nail
corresponds to the table below. For each new
electromagnet they make, have them record how
many paper clips the magnet can lift at one time.
They should add these results to the table
Ask them what they can conclude about the
number of windings around the nail, the number of
paper clips lifted and the strength of the magnetic
foiled?
(more windings lift more clips, therefore a stronger
electromagnet)

wire picked up

149
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
This is called a controlled experiment because all
variables (nail, battery, wire type, paper clips) are
staying the same except the number of turns of
wire. In that way, you can attribute the differences
to only the wire turns.
a. Ask Learners to plot a graph of paper
clips (y axis) vs. # turns (x axis)
b. From the graph ask them to extrapolate
by extending the trend line in the graph to
predict how many clips might 100 turns
lift?
Ask them to predict what might happen if they use
a bigger battery and explain why?
Note: (If the teacher has access to a bigger battery
or more batteries in series- try it and see!)
(more voltage from the battery(s) will increase current flow
and therefore the electromagnet will be stronger)
Testing Design:
If the battery is hooked up to the coil in the
opposite direction, does it affect the strength of
the magnet? Have Learners test this with their
electromagnet and paper clips. (the current is
reversed but the amount of current doesn’t change-should
be the same magnetic strength)
Possible experimental extension
If the nail is changed from steel to galvanized
steel to copper does it affect the magnet
strength? Have Learners test this and report
back which is the strongest magnet.

Matter and Energy in Organisms and Ecosystems
Strand (Topic): Matter and Energy in Organisms and Ecosystems
Understanding how ecosystems rely on the exchange of matter and energy, helps humans to better care for the environment including the constituent plants and
animals.
Essential Learning Outcome: ELO 1: Analyse and interpret data to provide evidence for the effects of resource availability on organisms and
populations of organisms in an ecosystem
Clarification Statement: Emphasis is on cause-and-effect relationships between resources and growth of individual organisms and the numbers of organisms
in ecosystems during periods of abundant and scarce resources.

Learners should make the link between abundance/scarcity
of grass and the appearance of the cattle. When they do, ask
questions to bring out the cause-and-effect relationship
between the abundance/ scarcity of grass and the
appearance of the cattle.
The cow in picture a) eats more grass therefore it is fatter
and looks healthier than the cow in picture b) which has less
grass to each and hence is not as fat or healthy looking.
Cause: An abundance of grass
Effect: Cow eats more and gets fat and looks healthy.
Cause: Very little grass
Effect: Cow eats less and is thin and looks unhealthy.
The cause-and-effect relationship is a relationship in
which one event (the cause) makes another event (the effect)
happen.
Cause is the reason for a situation or change; why
something happens.
The effect is the result or the consequent; the outcome or
conclusion- what happened. The cause happens before the
effect.

Resource availability affects growth, competition, and rate of
reproduction. How resources become available in an
ecosystem directly affects several factors within and among
populations of organisms. Let us have a look.
165
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
● Population changes: Changes in the amount and
availability of a given resource (e.g.: less food) may result in
changes or limits in the population growth of an organism
(e.g.: less food results in fewer organisms).
● Individual organism changes: Changes in the amount
or availability of a resource (e.g.: more food) may result in
changes in the growth of individual organisms (e.g.: more
food results in faster growth).
● Competition: Resource availability drives competition
among organisms, both within a population and between
populations.
● Rate of reproduction: Resource availability also affects a
population’s rate of reproduction

Language Arts - Writing an expository composition explaining the effect of factors such as natural disasters on food webs.

Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Interdependent Relationships in Ecosystems
Strand (Topic): Interdependent Relationships in Ecosystems
All living things grow, survive, and reproduce because they exist with other living and non-things that help provide the food and other resources that sustain
them. These are interdependent relationships. No living thing can exist without these interdependent relationships, hence the necessity to study these complex
interactions.
Essential Learning Outcome: ELO 1: Construct an explanation that predicts patterns of interactions among organisms across multiple
ecosystems.
Clarification Statement: Emphasis is on predicting consistent patterns of interactions in different ecosystems in terms of the relationships among and between
organisms and abiotic components of ecosystems. Examples of types of interactions could include competitive, predatory, and mutually beneficial.
Grade Level Expectation: Refer to grade level expectations at the beginning of this curriculum document
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
Learners are expected to: After the Learners complete this worksheet, the class Biotic and Abiotic Factors
will identify and discuss the factor shown in each
Knowledge picture, their general importance in an ecosystem and
their classification as biotic or abiotic

● mutualism lucia-travel-tour/

Answer sheet
Retrieved from https://slideplayer.com/slide/4287887/
Any ecosystem has two distinct factors or components.
Worksheet
Abiotic components are non-living, including things like
rocks, weather patterns, or bodies of water.
Learners label their non-living category in their graphic
organizer ‘Abiotic’.
Biotic components are living, including trees, mushrooms,
and leopards. Living components must adapt to each other
and the abiotic components in their environment; this is the
fuel for change. Failure to do so spells extinction, meaning
the species no longer exists.
Learners label their non-living category in their graphic
organizer ‘Biotic’.

The teacher will then inform Learners that they will be

The teacher introduces symbiotic relationships using a video
or pictures:
A symbiotic relationship is a relationship between two or
more organisms living together closely.
Guiding question: What are the three types of symbiotic
relationships? What are examples of each?
https://youtu.be/QHSHrunOvMo?si=gUM6Hl25mCrS90d
f (5:42 mins)
[Recommendation: pause after each type of symbiotic relationship to
generate discussion]
Mutualism:
The teacher refers to the graphic organizer to identify
mutualistic relationships in the Learners' everyday lives and
then the examples seen in the video
Mutualism: aphids and ants, red-billed oxpeckers and herd
animals, anemones and clownfish, lichen (fungi and algae).
Mutualism: Here, the living beings benefit from each other’s
company. This is a positive, friendly relationship. A
mutualistic interaction comprises two species of organisms
that work together, each benefiting from the relationship. A
classic example of Mutualism is pollination, as the hungry bee
gets its nectar from the flower, and in return, the flower is
pollinated by the bee so it can reproduce.

Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
Retrieved from: https://widget-
club.com/i/a32d4cf8848d4d91a17a
Learners add examples of mutualisms to their graphic
organizer. Learners may give other examples that they know
from previous experiences.
Commensalism:
The teacher refers to the graphic organizer to identify
commensalism relationships in the Learners' everyday life and
then the examples seen in the video (algae and manatee,
bromeliad plants and large trees).
Commensalism: It is a one-sided beneficial relationship
between two living organisms, where one organism reaps
benefits like food or shelter from the other organism without
benefiting or harming it. The partners that profit from such a
kind of association are commensal, while the latter organism
is the host species. Example: Cattle egrets eat the insects
stirred up by cattle when they are grazing. The cattle are
unaffected, while the birds gain food.

parasite, benefits or profits while the other organism, the
host, is harmed. In such an association, the parasite is found
to live within or upon the host's body. Depending on the type
