Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
Predation: It is an ecological interaction in which one
organism kills and feeds on the other. The hunter organism
in this interaction is called the predator, and the unfortunate
organism that serves as the food is called the prey. Example:
wild animals like tigers, lions attacking the zebra, deer, cattle,
etc.
Retrieved from:
https://ambergriscaye.com/photogallery/060430.html
Competition: When two different species fight or compete
with each other for the same resource, this form of
interaction is termed competition. The animals could
compete for resources like food, support, shelter, space, etc.
Organisms are compelled to follow this type of ecological
interaction for their survival or existence. Such ecological
interaction could either lead to the extinction of one of the
parties or the extinction of both parties. Therefore, this
193
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
ecological interaction allows only the best competitor to
survive and pass this trait to their offspring, thus, maintaining
the community structure. For example, competition for a
berry will be fierce if only two species of birds from different
species eat it.

Class Activity
Based on the information we learnt in this lesson, we will
now engage in a guided discussion, where a scenario will be
given and you will determine the type of relationship
(mutualism, parasitism, commensalism, predation or
competition) being described, and briefly explain your
reasoning.
a) A bee feeds off the nectar of a sunflower. While
feeding, pollen from the sunflower clings to the legs
and body of the bee. When the bee lands on a
194
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
different sunflower, the pollen is left behind and
new pollen picked up. _______________
b) A cat is carrying a dead mouse as a hawk swoops
down to grab it. They both pull on the mouse but
the flapping wings of the hawk against the face of
the cat are strong enough to make the cat drop the
mouse. ________
c) Polar bears wander the ice looking for signs of
seals. Once they find a seal they will attack and eat
it.
Follow-Up Activity
The Learners in groups can write a blog on the types of
relationships in one of the ecosystems explored in the lesson
and their importance.
Factors that cause changes in an Ecosystem
We might think of ecosystems as something constant but in
reality, ecosystems are subject to change, just like everything
else on this planet. Let's discuss the different changes to
ecosystems, and the natural and human causes behind those
changes.
If you've ever seen a fallen tree lying in the road the
morning after a thunderstorm, you probably already have
some idea of how natural events can cause changes in
ecosystems.
A natural disaster is a weather-related event that causes
widespread damage to an area. Natural disasters are not
caused by humans.
● Wildfire/forest fire
● Flooding
195
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
● Drought
● Earthquake
● Volcanic eruption
● Tornado
● Tsunami
● Cyclone
● Disease
Natural disasters can fundamentally change an ecosystem.
Entire forests can be burned down by a wildfire or uprooted
by an earthquake, leading to deforestation. An area can be
completely flooded, drowning all of the plants. A disease
like rabies can spread through an area, killing large numbers
of animals.
Many natural disasters only cause temporary changes to
ecosystems. Once the event has passed, the area slowly
recovers: trees grow back, animals return, and the original
ecosystem is largely restored. However, natural causes of
changes to ecosystems can be permanent. This usually has to
do with long-term changes to climate or physical geography.
For example, if an area faces drought for too long, it may
become more desert-like. Or, if an area remains permanently
flooded after a hurricane or tsunami, it may become an
aquatic ecosystem. In both cases, the original wildlife will
likely never return, and the ecosystem will be forever altered.
Human causes of changes to ecosystems are almost always
permanent because human activity often results in land-use
change. This means that we humans will repurpose land
that was once part of a wild ecosystem. We may cut down
trees to make way for farmland; we may pave over part of a
196
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
grassland to create a road. These activities change the way
wildlife interact with each other and their environment, as it
introduces new, artificial elements to a natural ecosystem.
For example, animals that attempt to cross busy roads in
search of more food will be at risk of being hit by a car.
If an area becomes urbanized enough, the original natural
ecosystem may functionally cease to exist, and any animals
and plants that remain in an area will be forced to adapt to
human infrastructure.
Besides land-use change, human management can play a
role in ecosystems. You can think of human management of
ecosystems as intentionally or unintentionally 'tinkering' with
the natural function of an ecosystem. Human management
includes:
● Pollution from agriculture or industry
● Manipulating pre-existing physical geography
● Hunting, fishing, or poaching
● Introducing new animals to an area (more on this
below)
Dams and wind turbines, which we depend on for
renewable, sustainable energy, can disrupt the natural swim
patterns of fish or flight patterns of birds, respectively.
Pesticides or fertilizers from agriculture can wind up in
rivers and streams, altering the acidity of water, and in the
most egregious cases, causing bizarre mutations or death.
197

Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies

Additional Useful Content Knowledge for the Teacher: (any additional knowledge that the writers believe would be helpful for the teacher such as reading material at a lower
or higher grade level, links to curriculum documents for other grades)
Different types of ecosystems: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=alU4hASej3c
https://www.generationgenius.com/wp-content/uploads/reading-material/symbiosis-reading-material-grades-6-8.pdf
https://keslerscience.com/symbiosis-lesson-plan-a-complete-science-lesson-using-the-5e-method-of-instruction
In some feeding relationships, both organisms benefit; this is called symbiosis. There are some relationships where only one organism benefits; this is
commensalism. In the feeding relationship called parasitism, the animal that is fed on is called the host while the organism that feeds is called a parasite.
The parasite has a negative effect on the host and can make it sick.
198
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
The balance in nature can be affected by natural processes such as hurricanes, earthquakes, volcanic eruptions, droughts and floods, and also by
activities of humans. Pollution of air and water resources, deforestation and excavations of portions of the earth are some of the ways in which humans
destroy the balance of nature.

Opportunities for Subject Integration:
• Mathematics - Collecting data showing the examples of the various feeding relationships in a community or ecosystem.
o Representing data collected in tables or graphs.
• Art and Craft - Designing posters, charts depicting the various feeding relationships.
• Language Arts - Writing an expository piece about the different kinds of feeding relationships - parasitism, commensalism, mutualism.
• Describing one type of feeding relationship.
• Health and Family - Writing about healthy habits one should engage in to prevent and treat various parasitic diseases.
• Social Studies: how do invasive species and human interactions upset the balance of interdependence in ecosystems?

Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Energy
Strand (Topic): Energy
The study of energy is most relevant given the increasing demands humans put on natural resources to power our technological needs. The knowledge of
potential energy sources and how we interconvert energy positions our Learners to be innovative as they look to a comfortable sustainable future.
Essential Learning Outcome: ELO 1: Construct and interpret graphical displays of data to describe the relationships of kinetic energy to the mass
of an object and to the speed of an object.
Clarification Statement: Emphasis is on descriptive relationships between kinetic energy and mass separately from kinetic energy and speed. Examples could
include riding a bicycle at different speeds, rolling different sizes of rocks downhill, and getting hit by a whiffle ball versus a tennis ball.
Grade Level Expectation: Refer to grade level expectations at the beginning of this curriculum document.
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies

kinetic energy: Demonstration/Group Activity

o Potential Energy volume), table top, bucket half filled with water.
o Mass

● Account for changes in kinetic energy
when the mass and speed of an object are
varied and vice versa.

We also know that as the ball falls, the potential
energy gets converted to energy of motion called
kinetic energy. When do you think the kinetic
energy is the highest? (Near the bottom just as the ball
hits the water.)
Where does the energy go after that?
(water get energy of motion as it moves)
Do you think all of the energy goes into moving
the water? (maybe yes)
Actually, any time we have a collision of two
objects (in this case, a ball and water) some energy is
lost in the form of heat given up to the water. Try
clapping your hands rapidly. After a few moments
of those collisions, you will feel your hands get
warmer as they absorb some of the kinetic energy
from that collision!
Demonstration: What About the Speed of the
Ball?
I want John to come to front of the class. Now let
us have him turn around so he is facing away from
me. I have a ping pong ball that I am going to toss
at John’s back. John did you feel the ball striking
your back? (yes but only gently) So John, you could
feel the energy as it impacted your back but it was
very small, correct? (Yes teacher).
John, what will it feel like if I throw the ping pong
ball at your back as hard as I can? (It wouldn’t hurt
because it so small but the impact would be much
greater than when you tossed the ping pong ball.
You wouldn’t want me to throw a cricket ball at
your back would you John? (no teacher) Why? John?

Concluding statements from Activities:
1. Kinetic energy is the energy of objects that
are in motion.
2. Potential energy is stored energy, ready to
be converted into another form
2. Kinetic energy and velocity (Ans: A) 3. As the mass of an object increases, the
kinetic energy of the object increases.
4. As the speed of an object increases, the
kinetic energy of the object increases.
5. Mass and kinetic energy have a direct or
proportional relationship.
One More Example: A Scenario
Which is heavier (has more mass) a cricket ball or a
basketball? (basketball)
When someone throws a cricket ball to you and
you catch it, it takes very little force from you in
the opposite direction to keep you from falling
over. If someone then throws you a basketball, it
requires more force from you in the opposite
direction to maintain your stance.

opposite direction. I may not have enough force, and may
fall over.)
So, the kinetic energy that something possesses as
it moves, depends on two things: the mass of
the object and the speed with which it is
moving toward you. In both cases the kinetic
energy is said to be directly related to the mass and
directly related to the speed.
Equation for Kinetic Energy:
Kinetic Energy can be found by using the formula:
1
𝐾𝐸 = 𝑚𝑣2
2
Where KE represents the Kinetic Energy; m
represents the mass of the object possessing kinetic
energy; v represents the speed or velocity of the
object and needs to be squared (multiplied by itself)
in the formula.
Example:
Consider a boy/girl rolling down a hill on his
bicycle. Together, they have a mass of 50 kg. Near
the bottom of the hill, the boy/girl has a speed or
velocity of 10 m/s. To calculate the Kinetic energy
at the bottom of the hill,

1. multiply his mass (m) by : 50 ✕ = 25
2 2
2. square the speed/velocity (v): 102 = 100
3. Multiply the answers in 1 and 2 to get the
Kinetic Energy of the boy/girl on a bicycle
going down the hill:
KE = 25 ✕ 100 = 2500 Joules
[Joule (J) is the Standard Unit of Energy]

We will now discuss the trend. When the mass
increases, the kinetic energy also increases by the
same factor - a constant that is multiplied by the
quantity to cause the increase or decrease. This type
of relationship is called direct proportion. When one
quantity increases, the other quantity increases.
Conversely, when one quantity decreases, the other
quantity also decreases.
Ask Learners to determine the amount of kinetic
energy possessed by objects of specific masses,
travelling at the same speed, using the graph. For
instance, what is the kinetic energy of an object of
mass 60 kg? (Learners should draw a line from 60 kg up
to the trendline and then read the horizontal axis (kinetic
energy) See picture below :)

Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
the graphs. Kinetic energy increases at a faster rate than
the speed increases. However, kinetic energy increases at the
same rate as the mass of the object increases. The shape is
different because when the speed increases by multiplying by a
constant, the kinetic energy increases by multiplying the
previous value by the square of that constant! The same
applies for division.
A similar exercise can be done with Learners on
this graph. Ask Learners to predict values and
verify on the graph. Then extend to values that are
related to the trend of the graph, but are not points
plotted on the graph.
Additional Useful Content Knowledge for the Teacher:
Definition of terms (source - britannica.com/science):
Mass: Mass is the amount of matter in an object. Its SI unit is the kilogram, but it is also measured in grams, pounds and tonnes. Another definition is an
object’s resistance to a change in position or speed in response to a force. This resistance is called inertia.
𝑑𝑖𝑠𝑡𝑎𝑛𝑐𝑒
Speed: Speed is defined as the rate of change of position of an object in any direction. Speed has the formula: 𝑠𝑝𝑒𝑒𝑑 = . If distance is measured in
𝑡𝑖𝑚𝑒
metres and time in seconds, then the unit of speed is metres per second (m/s). If distance is measured in kilometres and time measured in hours, then the unit
of speed after calculation is kilometres per hour (km/h).
Energy: Energy is the capacity of an object to do work. Work in this definition refers to a physical quantity which is the measure of energy transfer that occurs
when an object is moved over a distance by an external force.
Kinetic energy: Kinetic Energy is the form of energy that an object or particle has by reason of its motion. It is a property of a moving object or particle and it
is dependent on an object’s speed and mass.
Potential energy: Potential energy is stored energy that depends upon the relative position of various parts of the system. Example: A spring will have more
potential energy when compressed or stretched; a mango will have more potential energy the higher it is in the tree. Potential energy can also be considered
as energy that is ready to be converted into another form.
219

Opportunities for Subject Integration:
• Mathematics:
○ Plotting points on graphs (same skill used when drawing line graphs in statistics), and analyzing data on graphs.
○ Algebra: Substituting numbers into formulas: Learners apply this when calculating energy
○ Statistics: Analyze data sets for trends and patterns.
● Literacy:
○ Vocabulary
○ Report writing
○ Reflective writing in journals/portfolios
○ Writing, speaking, listening when presenting and analysing data from experiments.
● Visual Arts:
○ Infographics: Have Learners create visually appealing infographics that explain the relationship between kinetic energy, mass, and speed.
○ Visualization Techniques: Principles of design and aesthetics to enhance the clarity and impact of data visualizations.
● Information Technology:
○ Use of graphing tools (like Excel)to create and interpret graphical displays of data
Elements from Local Culture, Technology, TVET, Environment that are integrated:
Technology: Use of simulations to collect data and form conclusions. Phet Interactive simulations from the University of Colorado. PhET: Free
online physics, chemistry, biology, earth science and math simulations (colorado.edu)
Local Culture and Environment: Most Caribbean islands are mountainous and hence, roads have significant elevations. Learners can apply the lessons learnt
to explain why truck drivers apply more breaks when travelling downhill, or a filled bus will apply less brakes when going downhill compared to an empty bus.
The Caribbean has a high potential for renewable energy sources like solar and wind power. Understanding kinetic energy can help Learners appreciate how
wind turbines convert wind energy into electricity. This knowledge can inspire innovations in sustainable energy solutions tailored to the region.

Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Essential Learning Outcome: ELO 2: Develop a model to describe that when the arrangement of objects interacting at a distance changes,
different amounts of potential energy are stored in the system.
Clarification Statement: Emphasis is on relative amounts of potential energy, not on calculations of potential energy. Examples of objects within systems
interacting at varying distances could include: the Earth and either a roller coaster cart at varying positions on a hill or objects at varying heights on shelves,
changing the direction/orientation of a magnet, and a balloon with static electrical charge being brought closer to a classmate’s hair. Examples of models could
include representations, diagrams, pictures, and written descriptions of systems.
Assessment Boundary: Assessment is limited to two objects and electric, magnetic, and gravitational interactions.
Grade Level Expectation: Refer to grade level expectations at the beginning of this curriculum document.

Labelling Energy Transitions: Roller Coaster
Materials: 6 identical books, three pieces of hard
Use this drawing and put in the correct explanations. cardboard or wood to create a plank the same size,
three identical toy cars, a measuring stick, tape or
ruler, chalk)
Approach: Have Learners release the cars from
the same point at the top of the ramps. Ask them
which ramp appeared to have given the car the
greatest speed? (The tallest ramp). Since speed is a
measure of distance-the ramp length-per unit
time…how could they measure that speed of those
cars? (By timing the car over the distance from the top of the
ramp to the bottom.)
Ask Learners to use chalk to mark the distance
from the bottom of the ramp to the car’s resting
place. They can enter the distance in cm in the
table below. Based on those measures, which ramp
had the car traveling the furthest from the bottom
of the ramp? (the tallest ramp)

How Roller Coasters Use Energy.

232
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
https://contrib.pbslearningmedia.org/WGBH/con
v16/conv16-int-rollercoaster/index.html
A roller coaster ride is a thrilling experience which
involves a wealth of science. The ride often begins
as a chain and motor (or other mechanical device)
exerts a force on the train of cars to lift the train to
the top of a very tall hill building the potential
energy as the height increases. The steeper the
gradient the faster the roller coaster moves. The
roller coaster track is designed so that the potential
energy from a high height is converted to kinetic
energy of motion as the car descends but as it rises
again it begins to gain potential energy once again.
Additional Useful Content Knowledge for the Teacher:
An object can store energy as the result of its position. For example, the heavy ball of a demolition machine stores energy when it is held at an elevated position.
This stored energy of position is referred to as potential energy. Similarly, a drawn bow can store energy as a result of its position. When assuming its usual
position (i.e., when not drawn), there is no energy stored in the bow. Yet when its position is altered from its usual equilibrium position, the bow can store energy
due to its position. This stored energy of position is referred to as potential energy. Potential energy is the stored energy of position possessed by an object.
Retrieved from https://eschooltoday.com/learn/the-inclined-plane/

Review Discussion: Thermal conductors and
insulators
Question: Pots and pans are made from what
material? (Metals.)
Question: Why do you think they are made with
this material? (Metals conduct heat well)
Question: What material is used to make the
handle of the pan? (Plastic/wood)
Question: Why do you think the handles are made
from plastic? (They are not good conductors of heat.)
Thermal conductors are materials that easily
conduct heat energy. Thermal insulators are
poor conductors of heat energy. Metals are good
conductors of heat energy. Gases, plastic, wood,
cloth are all poor conductors of thermal insulators.
What Rate Does Heat Transfer in Different
Materials?

Materials Needed:
● wooden spoon
● plastic spoon
● metal spoon
● three cups/beakers (plastic or glass)
● Butter or wax
● Stopwatch or timer
● Tongs or heat-resistant gloves
● Safety goggles
Procedure
● Cut small pieces of butter or wax and stick
them at the same height near the top of
each spoon.
● Place each spoon into a cup or beaker with
the butter or wax above the rim of the cup.
Ask Learners to predict what will happen to the
butter or wax when the spoons are placed in hot
water.
Record their predictions.
● Fill the three cups or beakers with hot
water to the same level, ensuring the
handles of the spoons are sticking out and
239
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
the butter or wax is not in contact with the
water.
● Place each spoon (metal, wooden, plastic)
into a separate cup or beaker.
● Start the stopwatch as soon as the spoons
are placed in the hot water.
● Observe the butter or wax and note the
time it takes for each piece to start melting
on each spoon.
● Record the time for each spoon.
● Have Learners record their observations
and measurements in a table, noting which
spoon's butter or wax melts first.
Discussion Questions:
"What did you observe when the spoons were
placed in the hot water?" (The butter stayed on the
spoons initially)
"Which spoon's butter or wax melted first? Why do
you think this happened?" (The metal spoon)
"How does this demonstrate the process of
conduction?" (The butter or wax on the metal spoon
melted first since the metal was a good conductor of heat
whereas the others were insulators.)

Methods of Heat Transfer (The metal spoon is a good conductor of heat. When
touched, it conducts the heat from your finger, causing a drop
in temperature in your finger.)
1. Convection:
Allow Learners to observe a pan of boiling water
and how the water circulates.
Question: ‘Why does the water in a pan boil from
the bottom and then circulate to the top?”
Learners. I want you to show me with your fists,
what happens to particles when they are heated.
That is correct, they move more quickly and
further apart. Because they are further apart they
are less dense. We know from our previous science
experiments, that things that have more air
between particles tend to float on top of things that
are more densely packed (e.g. a cork or Styrofoam
in water). So now- can you tell me why would the
hot liquid particles rise in the pot? This process by
the way is called convection.
(This movement is called convection. It happens in fluids
(liquids and gases) where warmer, less dense areas rise, and
cooler, denser areas sink.)
Demonstrating Convection: If the teacher has
the materials conduct this demo for Learners and
alternatively, if technology is available “show” the
experiment.

Convection Current Experiment =- Lynda

Materials Needed:
● A clear glass or plastic container (large
enough to see convection currents, like a
fish tank or a large beaker)
● Hot water in a cup
● Cold water
● Food coloring (two different colors)
● Ice cubes
● Small plastic or glass jars
● A dropper or pipette
Procedure:
● Fill the clear container with cold water.
● Prepare hot (near boiling)water in a
separate container
● Add one drop of food colouring to the
bottom center of the clear container (red).
Place one drop of the other food colouring
(blue) to the left and another drop of the
same colour on the right bottom of the
clear container.
Ask Learners to predict what will happen in the
clear container if it was heated near the center..
242
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
Record their predictions.
● Carefully place the hot water in a cup and
place directly beneath the center drop of
the food coloring.
● Observe how the center food colouring
drop (red) rises and the side food coloring
(blue) sinks, creating visible convection
currents.
● Ask Learners to describe what they see
happening in the container.
Explanation: Convection occurs because the water
near the heat source heats up. Hot water is less
dense than cold water so the water around the red
food colouring rises, while the cold water around
the blue food coloring sinks. This creates a circular
movement of fluid, known as convection current.

Convection Currents: Land and Sea Breezes:
Sea breezes occur on summer days because land
heats up faster than the sea.
Warm air rises as it is less dense than cooler air. As
the warm air rises, cooler air from over the ocean
moves over land to take its place. It is this
Diagram retrieved from CXC CSEC Physics Jan
movement of warm and cool air that creates a sea
2012. breeze!
1. How does the design maximize heat transfer The opposite happens at night when the surface of
by: land cools faster than the surface of water. The
a. Conduction? copper tubing warmer air over the ocean rises and cooler land air
b. Convection? Cold water at the bottom flows out to sea to replace it.
of the collector, hot water at the top.
c. Radiation? Black surface at the bottom
of the collector and tubes are enclosed in
glass (greenhouse effect).
2. Why is the cold water tap placed at the
bottom of the tank? Cold water is denser than
hot water. The top of the tank will have the
warmest water and we do not want the cold water to
cool it down in the storage tank.
3. Why is the hot water tap placed at the top
of the tank? Hot water is less dense that cold
water so the hottest water will always rise to the top
of the tank.
4. What is the BEST colour you think the Hot-
water tank should be painted and why?
Black, because it is best at absorbing heat.

2. Radiation
“Why does wearing dark clothing make you feel
warmer in the sun than light-colored clothing?”
(By experiment, scientists know that black objects absorb all
wavelengths of light and convert them to heat. Conversely,
245
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
white objects reflect all wavelengths and therefore here is no
conversion to heat)
Learners may develop a hypothesis and use the
following experiment to test it.
Retrieved from
https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=DIfzIXmGth
0 (2:00 mins)
Experiment: Black coloured objects absorbs and
loose more radiant heat than a white object
● Black colour object absorbs and loses more
radiant heat than a white object.
● Take two metal containers A and B.
● Paint container A with black colour and
container B with white.
● Fill both the container with equal amount
of water
● To measure the temperature, fix two
thermometers in the container
● Note the thermometer reading
● Place the two container in the sun for about
15 to 20 minutes
246
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
● Again note the thermometer readings.
You will find that the temperature of water placed
in the black container (A) rises more than that of the
water in container white (B).
This happens because black object absorbs more
radiant heat than a white object.
● Now place both the container A and B in
the shade.
You will observe that the temperature of water in
black colour container cools down faster.
Therefore we conclude that black coloured objects
absorb and radiate heat more as compared to white
objects in the same time period.
Application of this is real life: A black car sitting in
the sun at the same time with a white car, will feel
hotter inside because the black colour absorbs heat
more than the white. When standing close to these
two vehicles outside in the sun, you will feel more
heat from the black car than the white.
Shiny bright surfaces are good reflectors of heat but
poor absorbers. Examples of this property for
reflection of heat can be seen in the shiny reflective
surfaces of cars, so that heat from the sun is
reflected instead of absorbed into the car, shiny
galvanized roofs in the Caribbean.
Objects that are good absorbers of radiant heat are
also good emitters of heat. They give off heat energy
through radiation.
247
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
Light colored shiny objects are poor absorbers and
therefore good reflectors of radiant heat.
Radiation is a method of heat transfer that does not
require particles to carry heat energy. Heat is
transferred in infrared waves (a type of
electromagnetic wave or radiation) from hot objects
in all directions. Unlike conduction and convection,
particles are not required for heat transfer through
radiation.
Learners can view the following video about heat
transfer: radiation.
https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=OYW7lR-

Greenhouse Effect: A Problem of Heat
Transfer
Learners, what is a greenhouse? (A place to grow
vegetables). Why is it a useful technology? (We can
grow things in a warm environment). Have you ever
walked in a greenhouse? What does it feel like?
248
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
(Steamy, war, wet). How does it stay so warm; are
they pumping warm air in? (No, hot air isn’t added, the
sun shines through the glass or plastic and the heat gets
trapped inside).
The greenhouse effect on earth is very similar.
When we burn hydrocarbon fuels like wood, diesel,
oil etc. one of the products is carbon dioxide.
There are other gases produced by industrial
processes that contain nitrogen and sulfur. If we
burn too much the carbon dioxide as well as other
industrial byproducts makes a blanket over the
earth. The sun shines through and warms the earth
but the reflected heat can’t escape because the
gaseous blanket holds the heat in. We therefore
have elevated temperatures at the surface; just like a
greenhouse! This has many detrimental effects!

Minimizing energy transfer: The Vacuum
Flask
A vacuum flask is designed to keep food or
beverages warm or cold. It does this by
minimizing the transfer of thermal energy from
outside to inside the flask and vice - versa.
Ask Learners:
What is a vacuum? (A vacuum is any space that has no
matter. This means that there are no particles.)
Show Learners a diagram of the parts of a vacuum
flask and discuss how each element of the design
minimizes the transfer of thermal energy. Discuss
the specific method of heat transfer being
minimized.

Can you identify the insulators?

reduced?
Why do you think there is a vacuum?

Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
Answers:
Plastic Stoppers and corks serve as insulators to
avoid heat transfer by conduction.
A vacuum has no particles, hence heat cannot be
transferred by conduction and convection.
The silvered surface is shiny and therefore reflects
heat back into the flask instead of outward. The
walls are thin hence limiting heat transfer through
conduction.
Thermos Engineering Project
Now that Learners know the three methods of heat
transfer, they will now explore methods of
minimizing heat transfer and use it to construct a
device.
In this activity Learners will apply scientific
principles to design, construct and test a device that
minimizes energy transfer.
Look at the following video for instructions:

Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=kfBcqhsaVJ0
(2:08 mins)
Learners are expected to test different materials
and come up with the best insulation to put around
their cup as well as the lid.
Two plastic cups are required: a smaller cup that
fits inside a larger one. It is intended for the
insulating material to fit in between the two cups.
Consideration for the lid design is also important.
Other materials:
● thermometer
● beaker
● Petri dish
● hot plate
● water
Examples of insulating materials Learners may use
include cotton, aluminum foil, cardboard, cloth.
Learners will test each materials’ insulation
property by placing it between a beaker of water
and a hot plate and heating the water for a specific
amount of time. The time for heating should be
the same for each material. Also ensure that the
water never reaches boiling.

Additional Useful Content Knowledge for the Teacher:
Notes on Methods of heat Transfer:
http://alkisites.vansd.org/dgray/Assignments/conduction_convection_radiation_reading.pdf
Definition of terms using in this ELO:
Thermal energy: Thermal energy is the energy due to the motion of atoms and molecules in a substance. Thermal energy can also be thought of a type of
energy since it involves movement of molecules. An increase in thermal energy of an object is directly related to an increase in temperature (ScienceFacts.net).
Heat: Heat refers to the flow of thermal energy. This flow of thermal energy occurs when there is a difference in temperature in a substance. Heat flows from
a higher temperature to a lower temperature. However, the term heat energy can be used interchangeably with thermal energy (ScienceFacts.net).
Temperature: Temperature is a measure of the kinetic energy of particles of matter, which can be measured by using a thermometer (Sciencenotes.org).
Conduction (Heat): Conduction is the heat transfer directly between neighboring atoms or molecules. Usually, it is heat transfer through solids
(sciencenotes.org).
Convection: Convection is the heat transfer via the movement of a fluid (liquid or gas) (Sciencenotes.org)
Thermal Radiation: Thermal radiation is the emission of electromagnetic radiation (infra-red rays). Heat transfer through radiation can occur through a
medium (particles) but it is not required.
256
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Vacuum: A vacuum is a volume which contains little or no matter (sciencenotes.org).
Retrieved from What Is a Vacuum in Science? Definition and Examples (sciencenotes.org)
Greenhouse Effect: The following page gives more information about the greenhouse effect and its consequences to living things on Earth.
Greenhouse effect - Understanding Global Change (berkeley.edu)
https://ugc.berkeley.edu/background-content/greenhouse-effect/

Elements from Local Culture, Technology, TVET, Environment that are integrated:
Solar energy is very popular in the Caribbean. It provides an alternative source of energy and helps reduce the region’s dependence on fossil fuels. Applying
heat transfer principles to solar water heaters helps Learners gain knowledge of how solar panels can be used as an alternative to electric water heaters.
In February of 2017, the UN launched the #CleanSeas Campaign with the aim of engaging various governments, the public and private sectors in the fight
against marine plastic. Sixty countries worldwide have responded including Caribbean territories. There has been a ban on the import of Styrofoam as well as
plastic products in this country. Hence, the activity where Learners provide insulation to disposable cups may prove useful, since Styrofoam cups, which served
as an insulated container for warm beverages are no longer sold in our supermarkets.
258
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Space Systems
Strand (Topic): Space Systems
Understanding the nature of our universe and the privileged place that earth holds, builds responsibility to care for our environment.
Essential Learning Outcome: ELO 1 Analyse and interpret data to determine scale properties of objects in the solar system.
Clarification Statement: Emphasis is on analysing data from Earth-based instruments, space-based telescopes, and spacecraft to determine similarities and
differences among solar system objects. Examples of scale properties include the sizes of an object’s layers (such as crust and atmosphere), surface features (such as
volcanoes), and orbital radius. Examples of data include statistical information, drawings and photographs, and models.
Assessment Boundary: Assessment does not include recalling facts about properties of the planets and other solar system bodies.
Grade Level Expectations: Refer to grade level expectations at the beginning of this curriculum document.

The Moon and Tides
Earth has one moon which is kept in orbit by its
gravitational pull. This moon is a natural satellite of
Earth. Also, when the sun and moon line up around the
earth, their combined gravity causes higher tides as the
water is pulled away from the earth’s surface.

qimg-c75266afb398b22afc6531f0f12b05b6-lq

Teacher note: If Learners have difficulty with the orbit
explanation above, return to the following analogy to help

satellites that are placed in orbit around the earth.
See: https://howthingsfly.si.edu/flight-dynamics/gravity-
orbit
How are mass and weight different ideas?
Our body has the same mass no matter where we are in
the universe. However, because planets and moons have
different gravitational pull, our weight is different
depending on which of these planets we visited. When
standing on a scale, a person’s weight is a measure of
Earth’s gravitational pull on their mass. On planets with
stronger or weaker gravity than Earth, a person’s weight
would change even though their mass stays the same.
How Much Would We Weigh on Different Planets?
Gravity on Earth is 9.8 m/s2
The table below shows gravity on other planets.
279
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
Share the table with Learners. Walk through an example
of how to calculate a person’s weight on another planet.
Sample Calculations
On Jupiter
On Earth, a person weighs 100lbs or 45.4 kg
How much will they weigh on Jupiter?
Gravity on Jupiter is 24.9 m/s2
This is a multiple of the gravity on Earth. How much
greater is the gravity on Jupiter?
24.9/9.8 =2.54 times greater
Therefore a 100 lb person x 2.54 = 254 lbs (~115 kg)
On Mercury
On Earth, a person weighs 100lbs or 45.4 kg
How much will they weigh on Mercury?
Gravity on Mercury is 3.7 m/s2
This is a fraction of the gravity on Earth. How much less
is the gravity on Mercury?
3.7/9.8 =0.378 times (a little over a third the weight)
Therefore a 100 lb person x 0.378 = 37.8 lbs (~17 kg)

Grade 6 Science Curriculum
History of the Earth
Strand (Topic): History of the Earth
Understanding the processes that have shaped earth over the millennia helps us to tap into our resources and predict /respond to natural disasters.
Essential Learning Outcome: ELO 1: Analyse and interpret data on the distribution of fossils and rocks, continental shapes, and seafloor structures to
provide evidence of the past plate motions
Clarification Statement: Examples of data include similarities of rock and fossil types on different continents, the shapes of the continents (including continental
shelves), and the locations of ocean structures (such as ridges, fracture zones, and trenches)
Assessment Boundary: Paleomagnetic anomalies in oceanic and continental crust are not assessed.
Grade Level Expectations: Refer to grade level expectations at the beginning of this curriculum document.

Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
San Andreas fault California; site of a conservative tectonic shift
When tectonic plates from around the world interact in such a way
that the oceanic plate (of higher density) is pushed underneath a
continental plate (of lower density) this process is called subduction.
The denser oceanic plate is forced down into the mantle where it is
heated and essentially recycled. The region where this occurs is called
a subduction zone.
In the activity below, we will try to clearly demonstrate how plates
can interact in different ways.
Student Activity: Modelling Plate Movements
Station 1: Divergent Boundary Simulation
Materials: Modelling clay, wax paper
• Roll two long strips of clay (each representing a tectonic
plate) and place them side by side on the wax paper.
• Slowly move the clay strips apart to simulate a divergent
boundary. As the plates pull apart, a gap forms between
them. Explain that this process is similar to how new ocean
floor is created at mid-ocean ridges.
288
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
• Explanation: When plates move apart at divergent
boundaries, magma rises from below to form new crust.
Over time, this creates features like mid-ocean ridges.
Station 2: Convergent Boundary Simulation
Materials: Two foam blocks or sponges
• Place the two blocks at the edge of the wax paper.
• Slowly push the blocks toward each other to simulate a
convergent boundary. When they collide, one block should
"subduct" (sink) under the other, demonstrating the
subduction of one tectonic plate beneath another.
• Explanation: At convergent boundaries, one plate is often
forced beneath another, leading to the formation of deep
ocean trenches and volcanic arcs. Alternatively, if two
continental plates collide (without subduction), they form
mountains, such as the Himalayas.
Station 3: Conservative or Transform Boundary Simulation
Materials: Two cardboard strips
• Cut two strips of cardboard and place them side by side on
the wax paper. These strips represent tectonic plates at a
transform boundary.
• Gently slide the strips past each other in opposite directions
to simulate a transform fault (like the San Andreas Fault).
Explanation: At transform boundaries, plates slide past each other,
which causes intense friction. The release of built-up tension results
in earthquakes.
Natural Disasters of Evidence of Plate Movements
Show Learners videos of natural disasters that show evidence of plate
tectonic movements.

Discuss how fossils provide evidence of continental drifts.

oceans suggests that these landmasses were once connected,
supporting the idea of Pangaea and continental drift.

Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Earth Systems
Strand (Topic): Earth Systems
Understanding systems on earth helps to take better care of our natural resources and protect our fragile environment.
Essential Learning Outcome: ELO 1: Develop a model to describe the cycling of water through Earth’s systems driven by energy from the sun and
the force of gravity
Clarification Statement: Emphasis is on the ways water changes its state as it moves through the multiple pathways of the hydrologic cycle. Examples of
models can be conceptual or physical.
Assessment Boundary: A quantitative understanding of the latent heats of vaporization and fusion is not assessed.
Grade Level Expectations: Refer to grade level expectations at the beginning of this curriculum document.

How much water is available for users on earth?
Have Learners set up a rain gauge at the school, collect weekly
rainfall volumes and plot rainfall volume in ml versus the month
in a histogram.
Have the Learners linked with another school in a neighboring

using social media to share rainfall data form another school. Do
comparisons of the monthly data and account for differences in
patterns (e.g. rainy seasons, prevailing winds, ocean currents etc.)
Creating a 3D model of the water cycle can be a fun and
educational project for Learners to understand how water moves
through Earth's spheres (atmosphere, hydrosphere, geosphere,
297
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
and biosphere). Below is a step-by-step guide to create a simple
yet effective 3D model using materials commonly found at home
or in the classroom.
Materials Needed:
• Large Styrofoam® or cardboard sphere (as the base)
• Modeling clay or playdough
• Blue cellophane or colored paper
• Small plastic trees, plants, or figurines
• Cotton balls or polyester fiberfill
• Craft glue, tape, scissors
• Markers or colored pencils
• Optional: Toothpicks, thin wire, or string for creating
connections
Steps to Create the 3D Water Cycle Model:
1. Prepare the Base Sphere:
• Use a large Styrofoam® or cardboard sphere as the base
for your model. This represents the Earth.
2. Represent the Geosphere:
• Landforms: Use modeling clay or play-dough to create
mountains, hills, valleys, and other landforms on the
surface of the sphere.
• Labeling: Use markers or colored pencils to label these
landforms directly on the sphere as "Geosphere."
3. Depict the Biosphere:
• Plants and Trees: Place small plastic trees, plants, or
figurines on the landforms you created with clay.
298
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
• Labeling: Label these plants and trees as "Biosphere."
4. Create the Hydrosphere:
• Water Bodies: Use blue cellophane or colored paper to
create rivers, lakes, and oceans on the surface of the
sphere.
• Labeling: Label these water bodies as "Hydrosphere."
5. Represent the Atmosphere:
• Clouds and Sky: Use cotton balls or polyester fiberfill to
create clouds around the sphere, representing the
atmosphere.
• Labeling: Label this area as "Atmosphere."
6. Show Interactions:
• Arrows or Connections: Use toothpicks, thin wire, or
string to create arrows between the different
components (geosphere, biosphere, hydrosphere, and
atmosphere) to show interactions. For example:
o Arrows from the atmosphere (clouds) to the
biosphere (plants) to represent rain.
o Arrows from the hydrosphere (rivers) to the
geosphere (soil) to show water infiltration.
7. Add Labels and Explanations:
• Use small pieces of paper or cardstock to write labels for
each sphere (geosphere, biosphere, hydrosphere, and
atmosphere).
• Write brief explanations of the interactions and attach
them near the corresponding arrows or spheres.
299
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
Rubric for Assessment:
1. Model Accuracy and Detail:
• Excellent (4): Model accurately depicts the interactions
between spheres with detailed and realistic features.
• Good (3): Model accurately depicts the interactions with
some detail.
• Fair (2): Model depicts interactions with minimal detail
and some inaccuracies.
• Needs Improvement (1): Model lacks accuracy and
detail.
2. Comparison Analysis:
• Excellent (4): Comparison is thorough, well-explained,
and supported by detailed examples.
• Good (3): Comparison is clear and supported by
examples.
• Fair (2): Comparison is mentioned but lacks depth and
supporting examples.
300
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
• Needs Improvement (1): Comparison is unclear or
missing.
3. Creativity and Presentation:
• Excellent (4): Model is highly creative, well-organized,
and effectively presented.
• Good (3): Model is creative, organized, and clearly
presented.
• Fair (2): Model shows some creativity and organization
but is somewhat unclear.
• Needs Improvement (1): Model lacks creativity,
organization, and clarity.
4. Collaboration and Participation (if group work):
• Excellent (4): All group members actively participated
and collaborated effectively.
• Good (3): Most group members participated and
collaborated effectively.
• Fair (2): Some group members participated and
collaborated.
• Needs Improvement (1): Few group members
participated or collaborated.

Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Essential Learning Outcome: ELO 2: Construct a scientific explanation based on evidence for how the uneven distributions of Earth’s mineral,
energy, and groundwater resources are the result of past and current geoscience processes.
Clarification Statement: Emphasis is on how these resources are limited and typically non-renewable, and how their distributions are significantly changing as
a result of removal by humans. Examples of uneven distributions of resources as a result of past processes include but are not limited to petroleum (locations of
the burial of organic marine sediments and subsequent geologic traps), metal ores (locations of past volcanic and hydrothermal activity associated with
subduction zones), and soil (locations of active weathering and/or deposition of rock).
Grade Level Expectations: Refer to grade level expectations at the beginning of this curriculum document.
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies

Comprehension Activity
Learners should be asked to read the following passage and
summarize the negative effects of mining.
“While there have been improvements to mining practices in
recent years, significant environmental risks remain. Negative
impacts can vary from the sedimentation caused by poorly built
roads during exploration through to the sediment, and
disturbance of water during mine construction. Water pollution
from mine waste rock and tailings may need to be managed for
decades, if not centuries, after closure. These impacts depend on
a variety of factors, such as the sensitivity of local terrain, the
composition of minerals being mined, the type of technology
employed, the skill, knowledge and environmental commitment
of the company, and finally, our ability to monitor and enforce
compliance with environmental regulations. One of the problems
is that mining has become more mechanized and therefore able
to handle more rock and ore material than ever before.
Therefore, mine waste has multiplied enormously.
As mine technologies are developed to make it more profitable to
mine low-grade ore, even more waste will be generated in the
future.”
Quoted directly from: https://www.safewater.org/fact-sheets-
1/2017/1/23/miningandwaterpollution
Distribution of Mineral Resources
The Caribbean region has been shaped by a number of tectonic
plate interactions
310
Grade 6 Science Curriculum
Specific Curriculum Outcomes Inclusive Assessment Strategies Inclusive Learning Strategies
(https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=z72DaFPMGwo ) that has
contributed to the formation of volcanic and coral islands. This
geology has inherently led to a limited number of mineral and
fossil fuel resources many of which have been exhausted.
Wood, coal and oil fuels traditionally used for heating and
cooking tends to be located in other parts of the world. This
means the Caribbean is heavily reliant on South America for
some of these necessities.
The interaction of the Caribbean tectonic plate with neighboring
plates has impacted the mineral deposits in the region.
“In the distant past, as North and South American plates rifted
apart, metal deposits began to accumulate in the rift basins,
which were later exposed, as a result of subduction on the eastern
edge of the Caribbean Plate. Within the Caribbean, some of the
most important deposits of bauxite and lateritic nickel, as well as
significant deposits of gold and silver, copper, zinc, manganese,
cobalt, and chromium, are hosted in the Greater Antilles. Massive
sulphide deposits formed in fore-arc basins as well as back-arc
basins due to volcanic activity, also giving rise to porphyry
copper and precious metal deposits in the more mature island
arc.
The various tectonic features listed above all formed as a result of
rifting and subsequent subduction and collision in the Caribbean
region. This provides a perfect setting for initial deposition
followed by uplift along with weathering to yield a variety of
deposits.” (Retrieved verbatim from:
https://caribbeantectonics.weebly.com/resources-in-the-
caribbean.html primary source:
Nelson, C. E., Proenza, J. A., Lewis, J. F., & López-Kramer, J.
(2011). The metallogenic evolution of the Greater Antilles.
Geological Act: an international earth science journal, 9(3-4), 229-264.

Largest Mineral Deposits in the World
The majority of mineral deposits seem to reside on large
continental plates rather than the subduction zones that populate
the Caribbean region.
